Linguistic and Legal Interpretation of Aman with Specified Numbers
Rules for aman with number specification and prepositional interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for interpreting 'fi' in aman grants and state the imam's authority in selecting specified individuals.

Core Idea
When 'fi' cannot literally include the speaker, it is interpreted as 'with' to give effect to the grant.

The grant of aman (safe conduct) is a critical tool in Islamic international law, and its precise wording matters. When a number of people are specified, the imam has the right to select them. But what happens when the language is ambiguous? This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq illustrates how Hanafi jurists resolve interpretive difficulties, particularly the preposition 'fi', which can mean 'in' or 'with' depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its literal problem
Read the source text and identify the exact Arabic phrase using 'fi' that creates ambiguity. Note whether the speaker can be part of the group mentioned (e.g., household, tribe, brothers, children).
For 'fi' with 'my brothers' or 'my children', the speaker cannot be his own brother or child, so literal meaning fails.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle of interpretation
When the literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible, the Hanafi school shifts to a figurative or contextual meaning (majaz). Here, 'fi' is reinterpreted as 'ma'a' (with).
This is consistent with the usul principle: 'Use the haqiqah if possible; if not, move to majaz.'
3
Determine the number of protected individuals
If 'fi' is interpreted as 'with', the speaker is not included in the number. So 'aman for me within ten from my brothers' means him plus ten others. If 'fi' is interpreted as 'in' (when inclusion is possible), the speaker is counted, so 'within ten from my household' means him plus nine others.
The imam does not choose who the ten are in the 'fi' cases? The text says 'al-bayan lil-imam' – the clarification is for the imam, meaning he designates the individuals.
4
Confirm the imam's authority
When the grant uses 'aman for me and ten others' without further specification, the imam chooses who those ten are. In the 'fi' constructions, the imam also clarifies which ten, but the total number includes or excludes the speaker based on interpretation.
The imam's choice is part of the implementation, not the validity condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعشرة فالأمان له ولعشرة سواه، والخيار في تعيينهم للإمام ، وكذا أمنوني مع عشرة» وإن قال «أمنوني في عشرة من أهل بيتي أو قال من بني أبي كان هو وتسعة سواه لأنه من جملة أهل بيته وبني أبيه والبيان للإمام. ولو قال في عشرة من إخواني» فهو آمن وعشرة سواه لأن الإنسان لا يكون من إخوانه فوجب أن يجعل حرف «في) بمعنى «مع» لتعذر العمل بحقيقة الظرف، وكذا لو قال «في عشرة من ولدي لأنه لا يكون من ولد نفسه قوله : (كانت خدمته أمانا (له الظاهر أن المراد أنه يكون أماناً له في حق العبد نفسه لا في حق باقي المسلمين كما ظنه بعض الفضلاء فاستشكله تأمل .
And ten: then the aman is for him and for ten others, and the choice of them is for the imam. And similarly if he says 'give me aman along with ten others' or if he says 'give me aman within ten from my household' or 'from my father's sons', then he and nine others are included, because he is part of his household and his father's sons, and the clarification is for the imam. And if he says 'within ten from my brothers', then he is safe and ten others besides him, because a person is not from his own brothers; so the letter 'fi' must be taken in the sense of 'ma'a' (along with) because it is impossible to work with the literal meaning of the adverb. And similarly if he says 'within ten from my children', because he is not from his own children. His statement: 'his service was an aman for him' – the apparent meaning is that it is an aman for him with respect to the slave himself, not with respect to the rest of the Muslims, as some learned men thought, and he found it problematic; so ponder.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides rules for aman when a number is specified. If a Muslim commander or someone says 'aman for me and ten others', the imam has the right to choose who those ten are. If the phrase uses 'fi' (in) with a group like 'my household' or 'my father's sons', the person himself is included in the number (so he plus nine others). But if he says 'fi' with 'my brothers', the word 'fi' is interpreted as 'with' because a person is not his own brother, so the total becomes him plus ten brothers. Similarly for children. The final sentence is a note correcting a misunderstanding: when a Muslim slave serves his harbi master, the aman from that service applies only to the slave himself, not to other Muslims. This corrects an earlier misinterpretation.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option or right of choice; here the imam may choose the ten individuals.
في (fi): A preposition meaning 'in', but here interpreted contextually as 'with' when literal meaning fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'I have aman for me and ten others', who chooses the ten?
A) The person who gave the aman.
B) The imam.
C) The ten individuals themselves.
D) The qadi.

Key Takeaway
In aman grants with specified numbers, the imam selects the individuals; and the preposition 'fi' is interpreted as 'with' when including the speaker is impossible.

Imam's Options for Land Conquered by Force (Anwa)
Ruling on the imam's options when conquering land by force: division or leaving with kharaj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the imam for land conquered by force, explain the proofs from Sunnah, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The imam may choose: divide the land or leave it with kharaj – both proven by the Prophet and Umar.

This passage explains the ruling on land conquered by force (anwa). The Hanafi school holds that the imam has the authority to either distribute the land among the fighters or leave it with its original owners subject to kharaj. Both options are grounded in the Sunnah: the Prophet divided the land of Khaybar, while Umar left the land of Sawad al-Iraq with its people, with the consensus of the Companions. Understanding this ruling requires recognizing the imam's discretion and the rationale behind each choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
List the two permissible actions for the imam regarding conquered land: division among fighters or confirmation of owners with kharaj and jizya.
Both options are valid; the imam chooses based on circumstances.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet divided Khaybar; Umar left Sawad al-Iraq with Companion agreement. These establish precedent.
Sunnah acts (fi'li) are evidence for permissibility.
3
Understand the restriction to land
Division applies only to land, not movable property. Returning movable property to the conquered is not permitted by Shari'ah.
The imam can nullify fighters' claims to land by giving them compensation via kharaj.
4
Contrast with al-Shafi'i
Al-Shafi'i holds that land must be divided like movable spoils; Hanafi rebuts with Umar's practice and long-term benefit.
Kharaj provides continuous benefit for the community.
5
Apply the ruling
The imam's choice depends on need: division is preferable when fighters are in need, otherwise leaving with kharaj is preferable for reserve.
The imam must also provide tools if he returns the land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بخيبر وعدمها اتباع لفعل عمر رضي عنه بسواد العراق بموافقة من الصحابة ولم يجد من خالفه وفي كل من ذلك قدوة فيتخير . وقيل : الأول هو الأولى عند حاجة الغانمين والثاني عند عدم الحاجة ليكون عدة في الزمان His statement: 'What the imam conquers by force (anwa) – he divides it among us, or he confirms its people and sets jizya and kharaj', i.e., jizya on their heads and kharaj on their lands. And anwa means force (qahr) as in al-Qamus. And by this, the comment in the commentaries of al-Hidayah is refuted. So division is following his action (the Prophet) at Khaybar, and not dividing is following the action of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) in Sawad al-Iraq with the agreement of the Companions, and he did not find anyone who opposed him. And in each of these there is a model to follow, so the imam may choose. And it is said: the first is preferable when the fighters are in need, and the second when there is no need, so that it may be a reserve for a later time. And it is not hidden that division after taking out the khums is restricted to lands, because regarding movable property, it is not permissible to bestow [i.e., return it to the conquered people] because the Shari'ah has not permitted that. And regarding real estate, there is a difference with al-Shafi'i because bestowing it would nullify the right or ownership of the fighters, which is not permissible without an equivalent compensation. And kharaj is not equivalent due to its small amount, unlike the [right to] their persons because the imam can nullify their right entirely, either for a small compensation or by killing. And the proof against him (al-Shafi'i) is what we narrated. And because there is consideration for them: they are like tenants working for the Muslims, knowing that those who come after will benefit. And although kharaj is small immediate, it is great in the long run, and the expenses are borne. And it is the bestowal upon them of their lives and lands only, and the remaining division is for its continuity. And if he bestows upon them their lives and lands, he gives them from movable property an amount that enables them to work, to avoid the limit of dislike. His statement: 'by the aspects of agriculture' – the commentators said that this is not a linguistic interpretation, because 'anwa' is from 'ana ya'nu 'unuwan' meaning 'submission and humility' which is intransitive, while 'qahr' is transitive. Al-Fath said: Rather the meaning is 'he conquered a town while its people were in a state of anwa' which entails the Muslims overpowering them. And here the infinitive is placed in the position of the state (hal), which is not regular except in well-known expressions. And using the necessary (lazim) and intending the necessitated (malzum) is not in definitions but rather in reports. And the correct view is that it is a metaphor, because 'anwa' is well-known in the sense of 'force' among the jurists, so it is permissible to use it in definition. End. And what was said in al-Bahr does not serve as a refutation unless it is literal and not metaphorical, and al-Qamus does not indicate that. This is because the author of al-Qamus does not distinguish between literal and metaphorical, as some said, but rather mentions meanings collectively. End. And it seems that 'some' refers to Ibn Hajar al-Makki; we have already quoted his statement at the beginning of the chapter on tazir. I said: But it is narrated in the chapter on 'ushr and kharaj from al-Farabi that it is from the homonyms (addad): it is used for obedience and force. Similarly in al-Misbah where he said: 'ana ya'nu anwa' when he takes something by force, and likewise when he takes it peacefully, so it is a homonym. And Makkah was conquered anwa, i.e., by force. End. His statement: 'And it is the bestowal upon them of their lives and lands only, and the remaining division' – this sentence is found in some copies after his saying 'for it is great in the long run' and in others after his saying 'to avoid the limit of dislike', and that is correct.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on the ruling for land conquered by force (anwa). The imam has two options, both established by precedent: divide the land among the fighters (following the Prophet at Khaybar) or leave the land with its original owners subject to kharaj (following Umar's decision in Sawad al-Iraq with consensus of the Companions). The imam may choose based on circumstances. The text then discusses the rationale: division is restricted to land because for movable property, returning it to the enemy is not permitted. A dispute with al-Shafi'i is addressed: returning the land to its owners without compensation would invalidate the fighters' ownership rights, which is impermissible. Kharaj, though small initially, provides long-term benefit. The passage also includes a linguistic analysis of the term 'anwa', clarifying that in legal usage it has come to mean 'by force', even though its root meaning is 'submission'. The placement of a phrase at the end is discussed textually.

Key Terms
عنوة (anwa): By force; in Hanafi legal terminology, conquest by overpowering the enemy with military action.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on conquered land left with its original owners.

Benefiting from Booty Without Division: Permissibility and Conditions
Commentary expanding on permissibility with proofs and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on benefiting from booty without division, identify the proof from hadith, and explain the condition of need and its exceptions.

Core Idea
Benefiting from booty without division is mubah with need; without need it is restricted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a key fiqh issue: Can fighters use spoils before formal division? The source cites a hadith from Ibn Umar and discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether need is required. You will learn the relied-upon position and how it applies in dar al-harb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (using fodder, food, etc. without division) and its initial hukm level (mubah with need).
The ruling is not absolute; it is conditioned by need.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith proof: the narration from Ibn Umar about eating honey and grapes without exclusion.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates permissibility: the companions' practice, but the scope is debated.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the two views: one requiring need (preferred by later Hanafis), one allowing absolutely (based on a narration).
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine the mufta bihi position: need-based permissibility, supported by istihsan and the Khaybar hadith.
Cited from Fath al-Qadir and al-Hidayah.

الخيط والمخيط» كذا في الشرح. ولا شك أنه لو تحقق بأحدهم مرض يحوجه إلى استعمالها كان له ذلك كلبس الثوب فالمعتبر حقيقة الحاجة؛ ذكره في فتح القدير بحثاً، وقد صرح به في المحيط . والضمير في قوله «ينتفع» عائد إلى الغانمين فخرج التاجر والداخل لخدمة الجندي بأجر لا يحل لهم إلا أن يكون خبز الحنطة أو طبخ اللحم فلا بأس به حينئذ لأنه ملكه بالاستهلاك، ولو فعلوا لا ضمان عليهم. ويأخذ الجندي ما يكفيه ومن معه من عبيده ونسائه وصبيانه الذي دخلوا معه. قالوا: ولو احتاج الكل إلى الثياب والسلاح قسمها حينئذ . ولم يذكر محمد قسمة السلاح ولا فرق كما ذكر المصنف لأن الحاجة في الثياب والسلاح واحد بخلاف السبي لا يقسم إذا احتيج إليه لأنه من فضول الحوائج لا أصولها. وفي المحيط وجد مسلم جارية مأسورة له في دار الحرب في أيديهم وقد دخل بأمان كرهت له غصبها ووطأها إلا إذا كانت مدبرة أو أم ولد له فلا يكره لأن شيئاً. المدبرة وأم على تأويل الملك فتجب العدة ويثبت النسب. والمأسور فيهم لا يكره له أن يسرق أمته وسائر أمواله ولا يقتلهم لأنه لا عهد بينه وبينهم وأموالهم وأنفسهم مباحة في حقنا اهـ
Whether he dies in the middle of the year or at its end. Then know that whoever dies in dar al-harb, his share is only not inherited if he dies before division or before sale. But if he dies after division or sale in dar al-harb, then his share is inherited, as stated in al-Tatar Khaniyya. His statement: (And they may benefit from fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without division) due to what Bukhari narrated from Ibn Umar who said: 'We would find honey and grapes in our military expeditions, and we would eat and not raise it.' He [the narrator] gave it absolutely and did not restrict it to need, and he stipulated need in one narration and did not stipulate it in the other, and this is istihsan, so it is permissible for the rich and the poor. The first view is that it is common property, so benefiting from it is not permitted except for need, like clothing and animals. The other view: his statement, peace be upon him, regarding the food of Khaybar: 'Eat it and feed it, and do not carry it away,' and because the ruling is based on evidence of need, which is being in dar al-harb. The apparent [meaning] of their statements is that weapons are not permissible for him except with the condition of need, unanimously, and it is stated in al-Zahiriyya, even though the author made all equal. He gave food absolutely, so it includes prepared food and other, even allowing them to slaughter livestock and return their hides to the booty. The permissibility of benefiting from what is mentioned is restricted in al-Zahiriyya to when the imam has not forbidden them from benefiting from food and drink. But if he forbids them, it is not permissible for them to benefit from it. It should be restricted to when they do not need it; if they need the food and drink, his prohibition does not apply. It is restricted to the mentioned items because what is not usually eaten is not permissible for them to consume, such as medicines, perfume, violet oil, and the like, due to the hadith: 'Return even the needle and thread.' Thus in the commentary. There is no doubt that if one of them is afflicted with a disease that requires him to use these, he may do so, like wearing clothes; the consideration is the reality of need, as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir as a discussion, and it is stated in al-Muhit. The pronoun in his statement 'benefits' refers to the ghazis, excluding the merchant and the one who enters to serve a soldier for hire; it is not permissible for them unless it is wheat bread or cooked meat, then there is no harm at that time because he owns it by consumption, and if they do so, there is no liability on them. The soldier takes what suffices him and those with him of his slaves, women, and children who entered with him. They said: If everyone needs clothing and weapons, he should divide them at that time. Muhammad did not mention division of weapons, and there is no difference as the author mentioned, because the need for clothing and weapons is one, unlike captives who are not divided if needed, because they are among the superfluous needs, not the essential ones. In al-Muhit: A Muslim found his captured slave girl in dar al-harb in their hands, and he had entered with aman; it is frowned upon for him to usurp her and have intercourse with her, unless she is a mudabbar or umm walad of his, then it is not frowned upon because something. The mudabbar and umm walad, they do not own them, unlike a concubine, because with the aman contract he guaranteed that he would not steal or usurp anything from their property. If he does that, it is a violation. If he has intercourse with his mudabbar or umm walad, the warring party does not make it permissible for him to have intercourse with her until her idda expires, because they initiated intercourse on the basis of claimed ownership, so idda is required and lineage is established. A captive among them is not frowned upon for stealing his slave girl and other property, and not killing them, because there is no covenant between him and them, and their property and persons are permissible with respect to us. End.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the rules of benefiting from booty without division and the inheritance of shares for those who die in dar al-harb. The author explains that if a fighter dies in enemy territory before the booty is divided or sold, his share is not inherited by his heirs because ownership was not yet established. If he dies after division or sale, the share becomes his property and is inherited. The permissibility of using fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without division is supported by a hadith from Ibn Umar, who reported that the companions used honey and grapes from spoils without formal division. There is a difference between two narrations: one requires need, the other does not. The preferred view (istihsan) is that it is permissible for both rich and poor, but the underlying principle is that common property cannot be used without need unless there is a specific legal concession. The commentator then clarifies that weapons are only allowed when needed, and food includes both prepared and unprepared items. If the imam expressly forbids use, his prohibition must be followed unless there is genuine necessity. Items not normally consumed, like medicine or perfumes, are not allowed unless needed for illness. The pronoun 'benefits' refers specifically to the ghazis (fighters), not to merchants or hired servants. The soldier may take enough for himself and his dependents. If everyone needs clothing and weapons, they are divided. The passage also covers the case of a Muslim who enters dar al-harb with aman and finds his captured slave girl. He must not usurp her unless she is a mudabbar or umm walad, because she is not owned by the enemy in that case. Intercourse requires idda. A captive among them is allowed to take property from the enemy because there is no covenant protecting them.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dār al-Ḥarb): Territory not under Muslim rule, where fighting is permissible.
غنيمة (Ghanīmah): Spoils taken from the enemy by force during battle.
مدبرة (Mudabbarah): A female slave who has been promised emancipation upon her owner's death.
أم ولد (Umm Walad): A female slave who has borne her master a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible because it is booty.
B) Makruh because there is no need.
C) Haram because it is theft.
D) Mustahabb because it strengthens him.

Key Takeaway
Benefiting from booty without division is mubah only when there is genuine need; otherwise it is restricted.

Selling Booty Before Division
Ruling on selling booty before division with condition distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling booty before division and apply the consequence (return of price to communal booty) and the post-division charity rule.

Core Idea
Booty before division is communal property — selling it is invalid.

The first ruling in this passage addresses a common scenario among soldiers: can a fighter sell his share of booty before the official division? The Hanafi school draws a clear line between pre-division and post-division sales, with starkly different consequences. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the statement 'لا نبيعها' (we do not sell it). The ruling is that selling booty before division is invalid (haram).
The reason given: 'because they have no ownership (of that specific item) and no necessity.'
2
Distinguish before vs after division
Note that the ruling changes after division. Before division, the price must be returned to the booty. After division, the rich soldier must give the price in charity; the poor soldier may consume it.
This charity rule applies only if the sale was made after division but the item was collectively owned before.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The sale is invalid because individual ownership of booty is not established until division. Until then, each soldier's right is a share in the collective, not specific items.
This is consistent with the Hanafi maxim: 'لا يتملك الغنيمة إلا بعد القسمة' (booty is not owned individually until division).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا نبيعها) لأنه لا ملك لهم ولا ضرورة إلى ذلك، وأفاد أنهم لا يتمولونها كالمباح له الطعام أطلقه فشمل البيع بالدراهم والدنانير والعروض فإن باعه أحدهم قبل القسمة رد الثمن إلى الغنيمة لأنه بدل عين كان للجماعة، وإن كان بعدها يتصدق به على الفقراء إن كان غنياً ويأكل إن كان فقيراً؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي التتارخانية : إذا دخل العسكر دار الحرب فصاد رجل منهم من الصيد بازياً أو صقراً أو ظبياً أو صاد سمكة كبيرة من البحر أو أصاب عسلاً في جبال لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب جواهر من ياقوت وفيروزج وزمرد من معدن لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب معدن ذهب أو فضة أو رصاص أو حديد مما لا يملكه أهل الحرب سوى الحشيش والماء، فإن جميع ذلك يكون مشتركاً بينه وبين أهل العسكر فلا يختص به الآخذ. فإن كان الآخذ باعه من التجار يقف على إجازة الأمير ثم الإمام ينظر في ذلك، فإن كان المبيع قائماً والثمن أنفع للعسكر من المبيع أجاز البيع وأخذ الثمن ورده في الغنيمة وقسمه بين الغانمين، وإن كان المبيع أنفع لهم من الثمن فسخ البيع واسترد المبيع وجعله في الغنيمة، وإن لم يكن قائماً . يجيز بيعه ويأخذ ثمنه ويرده في الغنيمة وهذا كله استحسان، والقياس أن لا تعمل الإجازة بعد الهلاك. ولو أن رجلاً من الجند حش الحشيش في دار الحرب أو استسقى الماء ويبيعه من العسكر أو التجار كان بيعه جائزاً وكان الثمن طيباً له، ولو أخذ جندي خشباً فعمل منه قصاعاً ثم أخرجها إلى دار الإسلام فإن الإمام يأخذ ذلك منه ثم يعطيه قيمة ما زاد الصنعة فيه إن شاء وإن شاء باعه وقسم الثمن على قيمة هذا الخشب غير معمول وعلى قيمته معمولاً، فما أصاب غير المعمول كان في الغنيمة وما أصاب المعمول من ذلك يكون للعامل. ولا يصير المصنوع ملكاً للعامل بهذه الصنعة، وإن كانت الصنعة على هذا الوجه في ملك خاص لغيره يجعل المصنوع ملكاً للصانع فينقطع حق صاحب الخشب، فأما إذا كان لا يضمن بالغصب فالصنعة لا توجب انقطاع حق المالك ألا : تری أن من غصب من آخر جلد ميتة وخاطها فرواً ثم دبغها فإنه لا ينقطع حق صاحب الجلد عن الجلد بهذه الصنعة، ولو أخرجت الغنيمة إلى دار الإسلام فأخذ آخر منها خشباً وجعله قصاعاً أو غيرها فإنه يضمن المبيع من ينتفع بصيغة المجهول والظرف بعده نائب الفاعل قوله : (والمأسور فيهم لا يكره له أن يسرق أمته الخ) الظاهر أن في هذه العبارة سقطاً أو تحريفاً فليراجع المحيط .
His statement: (And we do not sell it) because they have no ownership of it and no need to do so, and it indicates that they do not take it as property, like the food that is permissible for him. He gave it absolutely, so it includes sale for dirhams, dinars, and goods. If one of them sells it before division, the price is returned to the booty because it is a substitute for an asset that belonged to the group. If it is after division, he gives it in charity to the poor if he is rich, and eats it if he is poor; thus in al-Muhit. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: When the army enters dar al-harb, and a man from them catches from the hunt a falcon, a hawk, a gazelle, or catches a large fish from the sea, or finds honey in mountains not owned by the people of war, or finds jewels of ruby, turquoise, emerald from a mine not owned by the people of war, or finds a mine of gold, silver, lead, or iron that is not owned by the people of war, aside from grass and water, then all of that is shared between him and the people of the army, so the taker does not exclusively own it. If the taker sells it to merchants, it is suspended upon the permission of the amir. Then the imam looks into it: if the sold item is still extant and the price is more beneficial for the army than the sold item, he permits the sale, takes the price, returns it to the booty, and divides it among the ghazis. If the sold item is more beneficial to them than the price, he annuls the sale, retrieves the sold item, and places it in the booty. If it is not extant, he permits its sale, takes its price, and returns it to the booty. All of this is istihsan, and the qiyas is that permission does not operate after destruction. And if a man of the soldiers gathered grass in dar al-harb or drew water and sold it to the army or merchants, his sale is valid and the price is lawful (tayyib) for him. And if a soldier took wood and made bowls from it, then brought them out to dar al-Islam, then the imam takes them from him, then gives him the value of the increase from the craftsmanship if he wishes, or if he wishes, he sells them and divides the price according to the value of this wood uncrafted and its value when crafted. Whatever corresponds to the uncrafted is in the booty, and whatever corresponds to the crafted from that goes to the worker. The crafted item does not become the property of the worker by this craftsmanship. However, if the craftsmanship is done in this manner on specific property of another, it makes the crafted item property of the craftsman, cutting off the right of the owner of the wood. But if he is not liable for usurpation (ghasb), the craftsmanship does not cause the owner's right to be cut off; do you not see that one who usurps from another the skin of a dead animal and sews it into a fur then tans it, the owner's right to the skin is not cut off by this craftsmanship. And if the booty is brought out to dar al-Islam and someone takes wood from it and makes bowls or other things, then he is liable for the sold item used passively, and the adverb is the agent of the passive verb. His statement: (And a captive among them is not disliked for stealing his slave girl etc.) Apparently there is an omission or corruption in this phrase, so refer to al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'We do not sell it' regarding booty items that soldiers may use without formal division. The commentator explains that such items are not owned individually, so selling them before division is invalid. If sold before division, the price must be returned to the communal booty pile. If sold after division, the proceeds are given in charity by a rich soldier or consumed by a poor one. The passage then discusses cases where soldiers find natural resources in enemy territory: wild animals, honey, gems, and minerals (except grass and water) are considered communal property shared by all fighters. If a soldier sells such items, the imam has discretion to either validate the sale (if the price is more beneficial) or annul it (if the item itself is more beneficial). This is based on istihsan, not qiyas. Grass and water, however, are exceptional: a soldier who collects them owns them and may sell them freely. The next part deals with a soldier taking wood from booty and crafting it into bowls. The imam can either pay the soldier the value of labor and keep the crafted items, or sell them and distribute the price proportionally between the booty and the labor. The crafted item does not become the soldier's property merely by craftsmanship; this differs from cases of specific private property where craftsmanship can transfer ownership via the principle of 'takhallus' (making something anew). The passage ends with a note that the phrase about a captive stealing his slave girl may be textually corrupt.

Key Terms
الغنيمة (Al-Ghanīmah): Booty captured from the enemy in warfare.
القسمة (Al-Qismah): Formal division of booty among the soldiers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a soldier sells his booty item before the official division?
A) The sale is valid and the soldier keeps the price.
B) The sale is invalid and the price is returned to the communal booty.
C) The sale is valid only if the soldier is poor.
D) The soldier must also pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
Booty before division is communal; selling it is invalid, and the price must be returned.

Natural Resources in Dar al-Harb: Communal vs. Individual Ownership
Ruling on ownership of natural resources found in dar al-harb, with justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between resources that are communal (wild animals, gems, minerals) and those individual (grass, water) in dar al-harb, and explain the imam's discretion over sales by soldiers.

Core Idea
Wild game, gems, and minerals belong to the army collectively; grass and water are exceptions owned by the finder.

The second part of the passage lists specific natural resources that a soldier might encounter in enemy territory. The Hanafi ruling creates a default rule of communal ownership, with a sharp exception for grass and water. The imam's role in validating or annulling sales adds a layer of communal oversight. This lesson breaks down the categories and the istihsan-based reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List the resources mentioned
Identify all items listed: wild animals (falcon, hawk, gazelle, large fish), honey, jewels (ruby, turquoise, emerald), minerals (gold, silver, lead, iron), and the exceptions (grass, water).
The text explicitly excludes grass and water from the communal rule.
2
Classify each as communal or individual
For all except grass and water, the ruling is that the resource is shared between the finder and the entire army. Grass and water are owned exclusively by the collector.
The rationale: grass and water are abundant and necessary for daily use; strict communal control would cause hardship.
3
Understand the imam's discretion
If the finder sells a communal resource, the sale is suspended pending imam's review. The imam can either validate the sale (if price is more beneficial) or annul it (if the item is more beneficial).
This is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not qiyas. The qiyas would be that permission cannot operate after the item is destroyed, but istihsan allows it.
4
Note the qiyas vs istihsan dynamic
The text explicitly contrasts: 'وهذا كله استحسان، والقياس أن لا تعمل الإجازة بعد الهلاك' (All this is istihsan; the qiyas is that permission does not operate after destruction).
This is a textbook example of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'We do not sell it' regarding booty items that soldiers may use without formal division. The commentator explains that such items are not owned individually, so selling them before division is invalid. If sold before division, the price must be returned to the communal booty pile. If sold after division, the proceeds are given in charity by a rich soldier or consumed by a poor one. Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dār al-Ĥarb): Territory not under Islamic rule, where hostilities are presumed.
الاستحسان (Al-Istihsān): Juristic preference, a source of law in Hanafi school that overrides qiyas in certain cases.

Craftsmanship on Communal Booty Wood
Ruling on craftsmanship from communal booty, with qiyas to ghasb case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a soldier does not own the crafted items from booty wood, and apply the compensation rule based on the ghasb analogy.

Core Idea
Craftsmanship on communal booty does not transfer ownership; the imam compensates for labour and keeps the item.

The final segment of the passage addresses a soldier who takes wood from the communal booty pile and crafts it into bowls or other items. This raises a question: does the craftsmanship give him ownership? The Hanafi answer is no, based on a qiyas with the case of usurping property that carries no liability. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the distinction between communal booty and specific private property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the key question
A soldier takes wood from the booty (which is communal) and crafts it into bowls. Does he own the bowls?
The text says: 'ولو أخذ جندي خشباً فعمل منه قصاعاً' (If a soldier takes wood and makes bowls from it).
2
State the ruling and the imam's options
The imam can either take the crafted item and pay the soldier the added value of craftsmanship, or sell the item and distribute the price proportionally between the booty (for the wood) and the soldier (for the work).
The crafted item does not become the soldier's property.
3
Understand the underlying qiyas (ghasb analogy)
The text compares this to someone who usurps the skin of a dead animal (mayta), sews it into a fur, and tans it. In that case, the usurper does not own the fur because the original owner was not liable for ghasb (the skin was worthless). Similarly, the booty wood was not 'owned' by the soldier; it was communal, so craftsmanship does not transfer ownership.
The key distinction: if the original item was specifically owned property of someone who is liable for ghasb, then craftsmanship can transfer ownership (making the craftsman the owner).
4
Distinguish the two scenarios
If a person takes specific property owned by another (and is liable for ghasb) and transforms it, he becomes owner of the new item. But if the property is communal or not owned by anyone liable (like mayta), transformation does not transfer ownership.
The illa (effective cause) is whether the original owner had a right that is protected and claims compensation.

قوله : ( ولا نبيعها) لأنه لا ملك لهم ولا ضرورة إلى ذلك، وأفاد أنهم لا يتمولونها كالمباح له الطعام أطلقه فشمل البيع بالدراهم والدنانير والعروض فإن باعه أحدهم قبل القسمة رد الثمن إلى الغنيمة لأنه بدل عين كان للجماعة، وإن كان بعدها يتصدق به على الفقراء إن كان غنياً ويأكل إن كان فقيراً؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي التتارخانية : إذا دخل العسكر دار الحرب فصاد رجل منهم من الصيد بازياً أو صقراً أو ظبياً أو صاد سمكة كبيرة من البحر أو أصاب عسلاً في جبال لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب جواهر من ياقوت وفيروزج وزمرد من معدن لا يملكه أهل الحرب، أو أصاب معدن ذهب أو فضة أو رصاص أو حديد مما لا يملكه أهل الحرب سوى الحشيش والماء، فإن جميع ذلك يكون مشتركاً بينه وبين أهل العسكر فلا يختص به الآخذ. فإن كان الآخذ باعه من التجار يقف على إجازة الأمير ثم الإمام ينظر في ذلك، فإن كان المبيع قائماً والثمن أنفع للعسكر من المبيع أجاز البيع وأخذ الثمن ورده في الغنيمة وقسمه بين الغانمين، وإن كان المبيع أنفع لهم من الثمن فسخ البيع واسترد المبيع وجعله في الغنيمة، وإن لم يكن قائماً . يجيز بيعه ويأخذ ثمنه ويرده في الغنيمة وهذا كله استحسان، والقياس أن لا تعمل الإجازة بعد الهلاك. ولو أن رجلاً من الجند حش الحشيش في دار الحرب أو استسقى الماء ويبيعه من العسكر أو التجار كان بيعه جائزاً وكان الثمن طيباً له، ولو أخذ جندي خشباً فعمل منه قصاعاً ثم أخرجها إلى دار الإسلام فإن الإمام يأخذ ذلك منه ثم يعطيه قيمة ما زاد الصنعة فيه إن شاء وإن شاء باعه وقسم الثمن على قيمة هذا الخشب غير معمول وعلى قيمته معمولاً، فما أصاب غير المعمول كان في الغنيمة وما أصاب المعمول من ذلك يكون للعامل. ولا يصير المصنوع ملكاً للعامل بهذه الصنعة، وإن كانت الصنعة على هذا الوجه في ملك خاص لغيره يجعل المصنوع ملكاً للصانع فينقطع حق صاحب الخشب، فأما إذا كان لا يضمن بالغصب فالصنعة لا توجب انقطاع حق المالك ألا : تری أن من غصب من آخر جلد ميتة وخاطها فرواً ثم دبغها فإنه لا ينقطع حق صاحب الجلد عن الجلد بهذه الصنعة، ولو أخرجت الغنيمة إلى دار الإسلام فأخذ آخر منها خشباً وجعله قصاعاً أو غيرها فإنه يضمن المبيع من ينتفع بصيغة المجهول والظرف بعده نائب الفاعل قوله : (والمأسور فيهم لا يكره له أن يسرق أمته الخ) الظاهر أن في هذه العبارة سقطاً أو تحريفاً فليراجع المحيط .
His statement: (And we do not sell it) because they have no ownership of it and no need to do so, and it indicates that they do not take it as property, like the food that is permissible for him. He gave it absolutely, so it includes sale for dirhams, dinars, and goods. If one of them sells it before division, the price is returned to the booty because it is a substitute for an asset that belonged to the group. If it is after division, he gives it in charity to the poor if he is rich, and eats it if he is poor; thus in al-Muhit. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: When the army enters dar al-harb, and a man from them catches from the hunt a falcon, a hawk, a gazelle, or catches a large fish from the sea, or finds honey in mountains not owned by the people of war, or finds jewels of ruby, turquoise, emerald from a mine not owned by the people of war, or finds a mine of gold, silver, lead, or iron that is not owned by the people of war, aside from grass and water, then all of that is shared between him and the people of the army, so the taker does not exclusively own it. If the taker sells it to merchants, it is suspended upon the permission of the amir. Then the imam looks into it: if the sold item is still extant and the price is more beneficial for the army than the sold item, he permits the sale, takes the price, returns it to the booty, and divides it among the ghazis. If the sold item is more beneficial to them than the price, he annuls the sale, retrieves the sold item, and places it in the booty. If it is not extant, he permits its sale, takes its price, and returns it to the booty. All of this is istihsan, and the qiyas is that permission does not operate after destruction. And if a man of the soldiers gathered grass in dar al-harb or drew water and sold it to the army or merchants, his sale is valid and the price is lawful (tayyib) for him. And if a soldier took wood and made bowls from it, then brought them out to dar al-Islam, then the imam takes them from him, then gives him the value of the increase from the craftsmanship if he wishes, or if he wishes, he sells them and divides the price according to the value of this wood uncrafted and its value when crafted. Whatever corresponds to the uncrafted is in the booty, and whatever corresponds to the crafted from that goes to the worker. The crafted item does not become the property of the worker by this craftsmanship. However, if the craftsmanship is done in this manner on specific property of another, it makes the crafted item property of the craftsman, cutting off the right of the owner of the wood. But if he is not liable for usurpation (ghasb), the craftsmanship does not cause the owner's right to be cut off; do you not see that one who usurps from another the skin of a dead animal and sews it into a fur then tans it, the owner's right to the skin is not cut off by this craftsmanship. And if the booty is brought out to dar al-Islam and someone takes wood from it and makes bowls or other things, then he is liable for the sold item used passively, and the adverb is the agent of the passive verb. His statement: (And a captive among them is not disliked for stealing his slave girl etc.) Apparently there is an omission or corruption in this phrase, so refer to al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'We do not sell it' regarding booty items that soldiers may use without formal division. The commentator explains that such items are not owned individually, so selling them before division is invalid. If sold before division, the price must be returned to the communal booty pile. If sold after division, the proceeds are given in charity by a rich soldier or consumed by a poor one. The passage then discusses cases where soldiers find natural resources in enemy territory: wild animals, honey, gems, and minerals (except grass and water) are considered communal property shared by all fighters. If a soldier sells such items, the imam has discretion to either validate the sale (if the price is more beneficial) or annul it (if the item itself is more beneficial). This is based on istihsan, not qiyas. Grass and water, however, are exceptional: a soldier who collects them owns them and may sell them freely. The next part deals with a soldier taking wood from booty and crafting it into bowls. The imam can either pay the soldier the value of labor and keep the crafted items, or sell them and distribute the price proportionally between the booty and the labor. The crafted item does not become the soldier's property merely by craftsmanship; this differs from cases of specific private property where craftsmanship can transfer ownership via the principle of 'takhallus' (making something anew). The passage ends with a note that the phrase about a captive stealing his slave girl may be textually corrupt.

Key Terms
الغصب (Al-Ghaşb): Usurpation or wrongful taking of someone else's property.
الصنعة (Aş-Şan‘ah): Craftsmanship; the act of manufacturing or transforming raw material.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A soldier takes wood from the communal booty pile and carves it into a chess set. What must he do with the chess set?
A) Keep it because he transformed it.
B) Give it to the imam, who will decide on compensation.
C) Sell it and keep the profit.
D) Destroy it because it was made from booty.

Key Takeaway
Craftsmanship on communal booty does not confer ownership; the crafted item remains communal property.

The Horseman's Share with a Non-Owned Horse: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on horseman's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on entitlement to the horseman's share with a non-owned horse and apply the preferred view based on the achievement of intimidation.

Core Idea
Entitlement to horseman's share depends on intimidation, not ownership.

This passage from a Hanafi siyar text addresses a key question: if a soldier enters battle on a horse that is not his own—borrowed, usurped, or rented—and then loses the horse mid-battle, does he still get the horseman's share (two shares)? There are two narrations within the school. The commentary, citing Fath al-Qadir, leans toward entitlement because intimidation was achieved with the horse before it was recovered. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the conditions for the horse's fitness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The source presents two riwayat (narrations) regarding a rider who enters on a non-owned horse, then the owner retrieves it and he fights on foot. One narration denies the horseman's share, another grants it. The commentator indicates that the latter is preferred.
2
Analyze the preferred evidence
The preferred view argues that the key factor is intimidation (irhab) caused to the enemy. Since the rider had no hand in the retrieval, the horse's temporary presence for combat suffices, similar to if it had been destroyed.
3
Examine the condition of horse fitness
The horse must be sound and large (sahih, kabir) to be suitable for fighting. A foal (muhr) or sick horse does not qualify. However, if a sick horse recovers before spoils are divided, istihsan grants the share, unlike qiyas.
Istihsan prevails over qiyas here.
4
Apply the rulings to sub-cases
The passage lists many scenarios: retrieval before/after entry, horse gift, sale, leasing, etc. Each illustrates whether the horseman's share is given. The common thread is the presence of intention to fight and the horse's fitness at the time of use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب الغنائم وقسمتها
وفي
وأما إذا دخل على فرس مغصوب أو مستعار أو مستأجر ثم استرده المالك فقاتل راجلاً ففيه روايتان ولم أر ترجيحاً، وينبغي ترجيح استحقاق سهم الفارس الحصول الإرهاب ولا صنع له في الاسترداد فصار كالهلاك بخلاف البيع وقد كتبته قبل مراجعة ما في فتح القدير ثم رأيته قال بعد ذكر الروايتين : ومقتضى كونه جاوز بفرس لقصد القتال عليه ترجيح الاستحقاق إلا أن يزاد في أجزاء السبب بفرس مملوك وهو ممنوع فإنه لو لم يسترد المعير وغيره حتى قاتل عليه كان فارساً .اهـ. قالوا ويشترط أن يكون الفرس صالحاً للقتال بأن يكون صحيحاً كبيراً حتى لو دخل بمهر أو مريض لا يستحق سهم الفرسان لأنه لا يقصد به القتال . التتارخانية : لو زال المرض وصار بحال يقاتل عليه قبل الغنيمة فالقياس أن لا يسهم له . وفي الاستحسان يسهم له بخلاف ما إذا طال المكث في دار الحرب حتى بلغ المهر وصار صالحاً للركوب فقاتل عليه لا يستحق سهم الفرسان اهـ. وكان الفرق هو أن الإرهاب حاصل بالكبير المريض في الجملة بخلافه في المهر وفيها : لو غصب فرسه منه قبل الدخول فدخل راجلاً ثم استرده فيها فله سهم الفارس، وكذا لو ركب رجل عليه ودخل دار الحرب، وكذا لو نفر الفرس فأتبعه ودخل راجلاً، وكذا إذا ضل منه فدخل راجلاً ثم وجده فيها فإن صاحبه لا يحرم سهم الفرس ولو وهبها ودخل راجلاً ودخل الموهوب له فارساً ثم رجع فيها استحق الموهوب له في الغنيمة سهم الفارس فيما أصابه قبل الرجوع وسهم الراجل فيما والراجع راجل مطلقاً فاسداً . كالبائع في دار الإسلام إذا استرده في دار الحرب للفساد، وكالمستحق للفرس في دار الحرب وكالراهن إذا أفتكها فيها. ولو باعها ثم وهب له أخرى وسلمت كان فارساً، ولو استردها المؤجر أو المعير فملك غيرها بشراء أو هبة فالثانية تقوم مقام الأولى، ولو كان الأول بإجارة والثاني كذلك أو بعارية والثاني كذلك فالثاني يقوم مقام الأول، ولو كان الأول بإجارة والثاني عارية فإنه لا يقوم مقامه، ولو اشتراها في دار الإسلام وتقابضا في دار الحرب فهما ،راجلان ولو نقده قبل الدخول وقبضها بعده فالمشتري فارس والفرس المشترك بين رجلين يقاتل هذا مرة وهذا أخرى لا له إلا إذا أجر أحدهما نصيبه من شريكه قبل الدخول فالسهم للمستأجر اهـ.
أصيب بعده .
سهم
Chapter of Booty and its Division. And in it: As for when he enters upon a usurped, borrowed, or rented horse then the owner retrieves it and he fights on foot, there are two narrations and I have not seen a preference. It is preferable to prefer entitlement to the horseman's share because intimidation was achieved and he had no hand in the retrieval, so it becomes like destruction, unlike sale. I had written it before reviewing what is in Fath al-Qadir, then I saw it said after mentioning the two narrations: The implication of his crossing with a horse for the purpose of fighting upon it is a preference for entitlement, unless an addition is made in the components of the cause by a owned horse, which is prevented. Because if the lender and others did not retrieve it until he fought upon it, he would be a horseman. They said: It is a condition that the horse be suitable for fighting, i.e., sound and large, such that if he enters with a foal or a sick horse, he does not deserve the horseman's share because fighting is not intended with it. Al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the sickness is removed and it becomes in a state to fight upon it before the booty, then by qiyas he should not be given a share, and by istihsan he is given a share, unlike if the stay in the abode of war is prolonged until the foal matures and becomes suitable for riding, then he fights upon it, he does not deserve the horseman's share. The difference was that intimidation is attained by a large sick horse in some measure, unlike the foal. And in it: If his horse was usurped from him before entry, so he entered on foot, then he retrieved it in it, then he has the horseman's share. Likewise if a man rode it and entered the abode of war. Likewise if the horse fled and he followed it and entered on foot. Likewise if it got lost from him and he entered on foot then found it in it, then its owner is not deprived of the horseman's share. And if he gifted it and entered on foot, and the donee entered as a horseman, then he returned it, the donee deserves the horseman's share in the booty for what he acquired before the return, and the foot soldier's share for what he acquired after the return, and the returner is an absolute foot soldier. Like the seller in the abode of Islam if he retrieves it in the abode of war due to invalidity, and like the rightful owner of the horse in the abode of war, and like the pawner if he redeems it in it. And if he sold it, then another was given to him as a gift and delivered, he is a horseman. And if the lessor or lender retrieved it, then he owned another by purchase or gift, the second stands in place of the first. And if the first was by lease and the second likewise, or by loan and the second likewise, then the second stands in place of the first. And if the first was by lease and the second was a loan, then it does not stand in its place. And if he bought it in the abode of Islam and they received it in the abode of war, then both are foot soldiers. And if he paid the price before entry and took possession after it, then the buyer is a horseman. And the horse shared between two men, this one fights on it sometimes and that one other times, there is no share for either unless one of them rents his share from his partner before entry, then the share is for the lessee. After it, a share.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the entitlement to the horseman's share (sahm al-faris) of spoils when a person enters battle on a horse that is not his own—whether usurped (maghsub), borrowed (musta'ar), or rented (musta'jar)—and then the owner retrieves the horse during the battle, after which the person continues fighting on foot. The text presents two narrations (riwayatayn) without an explicit preference from the original author, but the sharh (commentary) suggests a preference (tarjih) for entitlement because intimidation was already achieved with the horse before its retrieval, and the rider had no role in its return. The commentator refers to Fath al-Qadir, which argues that the mere intention to cross into enemy territory on the horse and fight on it is sufficient grounds for entitling the horseman's share, unless an additional condition is introduced that the horse must be specifically owned, which is not required. The passage then lists several conditions: the horse must be suitable for fighting (sahih, large), so a foal (muhr) or a sick horse that cannot fight does not earn the horseman's share. Al-Tatarkhaniyya discusses a case where a sick horse recovers before the spoils are gathered: by qiyas (analogy) no share is given because it was sick initially, but by istihsan (juridical preference) a share is given because the horse eventually became useful for fighting; however, if a foal grows into a full horse in Dar al-Harb only after a long stay, it does not earn the share because it was not fit from the beginning. The effective cause ('illa) for the istihsan is that a large sick horse still causes some intimidation, unlike a foal. Numerous sub-rulings follow: if a rider's horse is usurped before entry and he retrieves it in Dar al-Harb, he gets the horseman's share; similarly if he rides another's horse, if his horse flees and he follows on foot, if it is lost and found. But if he gives away his horse and enters on foot, the donee gets the horseman's share for spoils before return, and the donor gets the foot soldier's share after return. The text analogizes these cases to a seller in Dar al-Islam who recovers the horse in Dar al-Harb due to void sale, to the rightful owner of a horse in Dar al-Harb, and to a pawner who redeems it there. If the horse is sold and another is acquired by gift, the new horse replaces the old; but if the first was leased and the second loaned, it does not replace it. Buying in Dar al-Islam with delivery in Dar al-Harb makes both parties foot soldiers, unless payment and possession occur before entry. A horse shared between two persons fighting alternately gives no share to either unless one rents the other's share, then the lessee gets the horseman's share. The passage concludes with a brief note 'a share after it', likely indicating the share is given after the retrieval. The commentary thus clarifies the Hanafi position on various scenarios of horse ownership and fitness.

Key Terms
سهم الفرسان (sahm al-fursan): The share of the horseman in the spoils of war, which is equivalent to two shares of the foot soldier.
فرس (faras): A horse used for fighting, conditionally required to be sound and large enough to be effective in combat.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference over qiyas; here used to grant the horseman's share to a rider on a sick horse that recovers before spoils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, if a soldier enters war on a borrowed horse that is later retrieved by its owner, what determines his entitlement to the horseman's share?
A) The ownership of the horse at the time of division.
B) The fact that the horse was used for combat and intimidation was achieved.
C) The soldier's intention to return the horse after the battle.
D) The length of time the horse was in his possession.

Key Takeaway
Entitlement to the horseman's share of spoils does not require ownership of the horse; it requires the horse to be used for combat with the intention of fighting, provided the horse is fit for battle.

Nafal After Ihraz: Permissibility from the Khums Only
Ruling with proof on nafal after ihraz from khums.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafal after ihraz, identify the source (khums) and recipient (needy), and explain the evidence and ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
After spoils are secured, a soldier's right is fixed; nafal is only from the khums and only for the needy.

This passage from Bahr al-Rā’iq expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqā’iq, clarifying when an imam may grant extra reward (tanfīl) to soldiers after the booty has been secured (ihrāz). The ruling is nuanced: before ihraz, nafal is unanimously allowed; after, it is restricted to the khums and the needy. We will trace the legal reasoning, the evidence, and the points of disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The base text states that nafal after ihraz is given only from the khums. Note that the matn does not mention the recipient's need; that is added by the commentary.
The ruling is a permission (mubah) but conditionally restricted.
2
Determine the timing condition
Nafal is permissible before ihraz (unanimous). After ihraz, it is restricted. The reason: after ihraz, the four-fifths belong to the soldiers; their rights are fixed.
The day of victory or defeat is considered after ihraz? The text says it is not permissible because the purpose of incitement is absent.
3
Identify the source of nafal
The imam can only give from the khums (the one-fifth reserved for the needy). The four-fifths are the soldiers' share and cannot be diverted.
The khums is the right of the needy, so nafal from it is like spending on those eligible.
4
Examine the recipient condition
The khums is for the needy (masākīn). Hence nafal after ihraz should be given only to needy soldiers. Giving to a rich soldier is not befitting (lā yanbaghī) and some scholars hold it impermissible.
The commentator notes that calling it nafal implies permissibility for the rich, but the preferred view is that it is makruh tanzih.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanīfa holds no nafal after ihraz at all; the Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad) allow it from khums for the needy. The Syrian school (Ahl al-Shām) allows nafal after ihraz more broadly.
The muftā bihī is the view of the Ṣāḥibayn, i.e., nafal from khums only for the needy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير/ باب الغنائم وقسمتها
بعد الإحراز من الخمس فقط والسلب للكل إن لم ينفل وهو مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه
سهم الراجل، ولو قال من جاء بألف فله ألفان فجاء بألف لا يعطى إلا ألف، ولو قال من جاء بالأسير فله الأسير وألف درهم فإنه يعطى ذلك، والفرق وتمام التفريعات في المحيط . والتنفيل إعطاء الإمام الفارس فوق سهمه وهو من النفل وهو الزائد ومنه النافلة الزائد على الفرض، ويقال لولد الولد كذلك أيضاً ويقال نفله تنفيلاً ونفله بالتخفيف نفلاً لغتان فصيحتان .
قوله : (وينفل بعد الإحراز من الخمس فقط) لأن حق الغير تأكد فيه بالإحراز ولا حق للغانمين في الخمس والمعطى من المصارف له والتنفيل منه إنما هو باعتبار الصرف إلى أحد الأصناف الثلاثة ولذا قال في الذخيرة : لا ينبغي للإمام أن يضعه في الغني ويجعله نفلاً له بعد الإصابة لأن الخمس حق المحتاجين لا الأغنياء فجعله للأغنياء إبطال حقهم اهـ . لكن تصريحهم بأنه تنفيل يدل على جوازه للغني، ومن العجيب قول الزيلعي لا يجوز للغني فإن من عمل فقتله فله سلبه كما لو قتله في الصف أو بعد الهزيمة. أما لو قال ذلك بعدما اصطفوا للقتال فهو على ذكر القتال حتى ينقضي ولو بقي أياماً قول المصنف : (وينفل بعد الإحراز من الخمس الخ) في المنبع عن الذخيرة: لا خلاف بين العلماء أن التنفيل قبل الإصابة وإحراز الغنيمة وقبل أن تضع الحرب أوزارها جائز، ويوم الهزيمة ويوم الفتح لا يجوز لأن القصد به التحريض على القتال ولا حاجة إليه إذا انهزم العدو وظهر المسلمون لأنهم لا يتقاعدون عن القتال حينئذ بل يبالغون بلا تحريض فيتضمن إبطال حق الغانمين والفقراء بلا نفع ولذا لا ينبغي قبل الهزيمة والفتح من غير استثنائهما بل يقيد فيقول من قتل قتيلاً قبل الفتح والهزيمة فله سلبه، ولو أطلق بقي فيهما ألا ترى أن عامة القتلى والأسارى يوم بدر كان بعد الهزيمة وقد سلموا لمن أخذهم، وأما بعد الإحراز فلا يجوز إلا الخمس إذا كان محتاجاً لأنه حق المحتاجين، ولا ينبغي أن يضع ذلك في المحتاجين. والمراد بالإحراز أن تقع الغنيمة في أيدي العسكر والسرية اهـ ملخصاً. كذا في شرح المقدسي لكن الذي في الزيلعي وغيره، تفسير الإحراز بدار الإسلام ومفاده جواز التنفيل قبل الخمس يوم الفتح والهزيمة إلا أن يقال إنه غير معتبر المفهوم بدليل ما مر ولما في شرح السير الكبير قال أبو حنيفة : لا نفل بعد إحراز الغنيمة وأهل الشام يجوزونه بعد الإحراز وما قلنا دليل على فساد قولهم لأن التنفيل للتحريض وذلك قبل الإصابة لا بعدها ولأنه لإثبات الاختصاص ابتداء لا لإبطال حق ثابت للغانمين وفي التنفيل بعد الإصابة إبطال الحق ثم أجاب عما ورد من التنفيل بعد الإحراز بأنه كان من الخمس . قوله : (فإن ظاهر ما في الذخيرة عدم الحرمة قال في النهر : ممنوع بل ظاهر في الحرمة كما قاله الشارح لأن إبطال حق الغير لا يجوز اهـ . وأما تعبيره بـ «بلا ينبغي» فلا يقتضي عدم الحرمة لأنه غير مطرد فيما تركه أولى ألا ترى إلى قول الهداية وينبغي للمسلمين أن لا يغدروا» وقولها «ولا ينبغي أن يباع السلاح منهم وقول المتن في الأيمان ومن حلف على معصية ينبغي أن يحنث) شائع في كلامهم
Book of Siyar / Chapter of Booty and Its Division: After ihraz (securing the spoils) [it is given] only from the khums, and the salb (spoils of a killed enemy) is for everyone if he did not make nafal (extra reward), and it is his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him. The share of a foot soldier... And nafal is the imam's giving to a horseman above his share; it is from nafal meaning increase, from which comes nafl meaning supererogatory above the obligatory. Also said for a grandchild similarly. And it is said 'naffalahu tanfeelan' and 'nafalahu nafalan' two eloquent languages. His statement: 'He gives nafal after ihraz from the khums only' because the right of others is confirmed in it by ihraz, and there is no right for the ghānimīn (those who took the booty) in the khums, and what is given from the khums is from the channels of expenditure, and nafal from it is only by considering it as spending to one of the three categories. Hence al-Dhakhīra said: It is not befitting for the imam to give it to the rich and make it nafal for him after the injury because the khums is the right of the needy, not the rich, so making it for the rich is voiding their right, etc. But their explicit statement that it is nafal indicates its permissibility for the rich, and amazing is al-Zaylaʿī's statement that it is not permissible for the rich... His statement: (And he gives nafal after ihraz from the khums...): In al-Manbaʿ from al-Dhakhīra: There is no disagreement among scholars that nafal before injury and securing the booty and before the war lays down its burdens is permissible. The day of defeat and the day of victory is not permissible because the aim is incitement to fight, and there is no need for it when the enemy is defeated and the Muslims are victorious... After ihraz it is not permissible except from the khums if he is needy, because it is the right of the needy, and it is not befitting to put it in the needy... Al-Zaylaʿī and others interpret ihraz as Dār al-Islām... Abu Hanīfa said: No nafal after securing the booty, and the people of Syria allow it after ihraz, and what we said is evidence of the invalidity of their statement... His statement: (If the apparent in al-Dhakhīra is non-prohibition, al-Nahr says: It is prohibited but rather apparent in prohibition as the commentator said because voiding the right of others is not permissible... And his expression 'lā yanbaghī' (it is not befitting) does not necessitate non-prohibition because it is not consistent...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof passage from the Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq. It addresses the permissibility of an imam giving nafal (extra reward beyond the normal share) to soldiers after the spoils have been secured (ihraz). The matn states that nafal is given only from the khums, not from the four-fifths share of the soldiers. The commentator expands by explaining that the khums is the right of the needy, and nafal from it is like spending on one of the eligible categories. He cites al-Dhakhīra's view that it is not befitting to give it to the rich, but notes that calling it nafal implies permissibility even for the rich, contradicting al-Zaylaʿī's prohibition. The passage then discusses the timing: nafal is unanimously allowed before the spoils are secured, but on the day of victory or defeat it is not, because the purpose of incitement is absent. After ihraz, only the khums may be used if the recipient is needy. The Hanafi position, stated by Abu Hanīfa, is that no nafal is given after ihraz, opposing the Syrian school. The commentator clarifies that 'lā yanbaghī' (it is not befitting) does not necessarily indicate prohibition in Hanafi terminology.

Key Terms
تنفيل (tanfīl): Giving extra reward (nafal) to a soldier beyond his normal share of booty.
إحراز (iḥrāz): Securing the spoils in Muslim hands, i.e., bringing them to Dār al-Islām or into the army's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible for the imam to give nafal (extra reward) from the khums?
A) Only before the spoils are secured (ihraz)
B) Only after ihraz, and only to the needy
C) At any time, from any part of the booty
D) Only on the day of victory

Key Takeaway
Nafal after ihraz is only from the khums and only for the needy; giving from the four-fifths or to the rich is not permissible.

Definition of Salb and Its Ruling as Ghanimah
Defines salb and establishes its ruling as ghanimah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define salb according to Hanafi custom and state that it is distributed as ghanimah, not automatically given to the killer.

Core Idea
Salb is ghanimah for all – the killer gets no special right unless the imam grants it as nafal.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of salb (the spoils taken from a killed enemy) and its ruling in Hanafi fiqh. The passage clarifies that salb is considered part of the general booty (ghanimah) and is divided among all soldiers, contrary to the popular notion that the killer automatically claims it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of war booty in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of salb
Find the description of salb in the passage. It includes the mount, clothes, weapons, and what is with the killed. Note that this is based on custom (urf).
Salb is defined by the common usage of the term.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on salb
The matn says 'Wa al-salb lil-kull' meaning it is for everyone. The commentator explains that salb is taken by the collective strength of the army, so it is ghanimah and divided equally among the soldiers. The killer does not get a special share.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Analyze the primary dalil
The hadith of al-Hubāb: 'You do not have from the salb of your killed except what your imam is pleased with.' This negates automatic ownership and gives discretion to the imam.
This hadith is ahad but preferred.
4
Reconcile the conflicting hadith
The other hadith 'Whoever kills a killed one gets his salb' is interpreted as nafal (extra reward) rather than an automatic right. This harmonizes with the first hadith.
The Hanafi tarjih mechanism resolves the apparent conflict.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that the killer automatically gets the salb based on the second hadith. The Hanafi school counters with the first hadith and interprets the second as nafal.
The practical consequence: in Hanafi, salb goes to the bayt al-mal; in Shafi'i, it goes to the killer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والسلب للكل إن لم ينفل) أي لا يختص به القاتل عندنا لأنه مأخوذ بقوة الجيش فيكون غنيمة فيقسم بينهم قسمة الغنائم كما نطق به النص وقال عليه السلام الحبيب بن أبي سلمة ليس لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك. وأما قوله عليه السلام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه» (۱) فيحتمل نصب الشرع ويحتمل التنفيل فنحمله على الثاني لما روينا قوله : (وهو) مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه) أي السلب ما ذكر للعرف. وفي المغرب : السلب المسلوب وعن الليث والأزهري : كل ما على الإنسان من اللباس فهو سلب وللفقهاء فيه كلام .اهـ. وفي القاموس السلب بالتحريك ما يسلب وجمعه أسلاب ودخل في مركبه ما كان عليه من سرج وآلة وما مع المقتول شامل لما كان في وسطه أو على دابته وما عدا ذلك مما هو مع غلامه أو في بيته أو في خيمته فليس بسلب. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان السلب عند المشرك عارية من صبي أو امرأة لأنه يستغنم مالهما كمال البالغ، وما إذا كان السلب ملكاً لمسلم دخل دارهم بأمان فغصبه المشرك المقتول لأنه ملكه بالاستيلاء فانقطع ملك المسلم عنه . ولو أخذ المشركون سلب المقتول ثم انهزموا فهو غنيمة ولا شيء للقاتل لأنهم ملكوه بالاستيلاء فبطل ملك القاتل ثم ملكه الغزاة وإن لم يدر أنهم أخذوه، فإن كان منزوعاً عنه فهو فيء لإثبات يدهم عليه بالنزع وإلا فهو للقاتل وإن جره المشركون أو حملوه على دابته وعليها سلاحه بخلاف ما إذا حملوا أسلحتهم وأمتعتهم عليها فإنه فيء. ولو وجد على دابة بعدما سار العسكر مرحلة أو مرحلتين ولا يدري أكان أحد في يد أو لا فهو للقاتل قياساً لا استحساناً ولو قال من قتل قتيلاً فله فرسه فقتل راجل راجلاً ومع غلامه فرسه قائم بجنبه بين الصفين يكون للقاتل فرسه إذا كان فرسه مع . غلامه بقرب منه لأن مقصود الإمام قتل من كان متمكناً من القتال فارساً وهذا كذلك، وإن لم يكن بجنبه في الصف فلا يكون له. ولو قتل مشركاً على برذون كان له لأنه يسمى فارساً، ولو كان على حمار أو بغل أو حمل لا يستحق السلب لأن راكب هذه الأشياء لا فارساً ولذا لا يسمى يستحق سهم الفارس؛ كذا في المحيط وبه علم أن ما ذكره الشارح عن المحيط بأنه قال الإمام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه سبق قلم، وإنما المذكور في المحيط فله فرسه والدليل عليه أنه قوله : (سبق (قلم قال الرملي : أي من بعض النساخ والذي في نسخنا من الزيلعي فله فرسه كما في المحيط
His statement: (And the salb is for everyone if he did not make nafal) meaning it is not exclusive to the killer according to us because it is taken by the strength of the army, so it is ghanimah and is divided among them as ghanimah is divided, as the text states. The Prophet ﷺ said to al-Hubāb ibn Abī Salama: 'You do not have from the salb of your killed except what your imam is pleased with.' As for his saying, 'Whoever kills a killed one gets his salb,' it is possible it was established by the Law or it could be nafal, so we carry it on the second because of what we narrated. His statement: (And it is) his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him. i.e., salb is what is mentioned according to custom. In al-Mughrib: al-salb is the plundered item. And from al-Layth and al-Azhari: everything on a person of clothing is salb, and the jurists differ. In al-Qāmūs: al-salb (with tahrīk) is what is plundered, plural aslāb. Included in his mount is what is on it of saddle and equipment, and what is with the killed includes what is in his waist or on his animal, and other than that which is with his servant or in his house or tent is not salb. [The rule] is absolute and includes if the salb was with the polytheist as a loan from a boy or woman, because their property is taken as booty like that of an adult; and if the salb was owned by a Muslim who entered their land with aman and the killed polytheist usurped it, because he owns it by domination, so the Muslim's ownership ceases. If the polytheists took the salb of the killed and then were defeated, it is ghanimah and nothing for the killer because they owned it by domination, so the killer's ownership is nullified, then the warriors own it even if they did not know they took it. If it was stripped from him, it is fay' because they placed their hand on it by stripping; otherwise it is for the killer. If the polytheists dragged it or loaded it on his animal while his weapon is on it, contrary to if they load their own weapons and goods on it, then it is fay'. If found on an animal after the army marched one or two stages and it is not known whether anyone had it, it is for the killer by qiyas not istihsan. If he said, 'Whoever kills a killed one gets his horse,' and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier while with his servant his horse stands beside him between the two lines, he gets the horse if his horse is near him because the imam's purpose is killing those who are capable of fighting as a horseman, and this is such. If he is not beside him in the line, he does not get it. If he kills a polytheist on a horse (baradhūn) he gets it because he is called a horseman; if on a donkey or mule or baggage animal, he does not deserve the salb because the rider of such things is not a horseman and does not deserve the horseman's share. So in al-Muhīt, and from it it is known that what the commentator mentioned from al-Muhīt that the imam said 'Whoever kills a killed one gets his salb' is a slip of the pen; what is mentioned in al-Muhīt is 'gets his horse', and the evidence is... His statement: (slip of the pen) al-Ramlī said: i.e., from some copyists, and in our copies from al-Zaylaʿī 'gets his horse' as in al-Muhīt.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-ruling passage. It defines 'salb' (the spoils taken from a killed enemy) and clarifies its distribution in Hanafi fiqh. The matn states that the salb is for all the soldiers, not exclusively for the killer, unless the imam makes it nafal (extra reward) for the killer. The commentator explains that the salb is considered ghanimah because it was acquired through the collective strength of the army, and it is divided accordingly. Two hadiths are cited: one where the Prophet said to al-Hubāb that he does not own the salb except by the imam's permission, and another where the Prophet said 'Whoever kills a killed one gets his salb.' The Hanafis interpret the second as nafal, not as an absolute right, based on the first hadith. Then the commentator defines 'salb' according to custom: it includes the enemy's mount, clothes, weapons, and what is with him (such as items on his person or animal). It does not include items with his servant or in his home. Various scenarios are discussed: if the salb was borrowed from a child or woman, it is still ghanimah; if it was owned by a Muslim who entered with aman and was usurped, the killer does not get it; if the polytheists take the salb and then are defeated, it becomes ghanimah again. The passage also rules on specific cases like finding a horse after marching, and the imam's statement about giving the horse to the killer. A textual correction is noted: the proper version in al-Muhīt is 'gets his horse', not 'gets his salb'.

Key Terms
سلب (salb): The belongings on the person of a killed enemy: mount, clothes, weapons, and what is with him.
غنيمة (ghanīmah): Booty taken from the enemy by force, subject to khums and division among soldiers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of salb?
A) It belongs exclusively to the killer
B) It is part of ghanimah and distributed among all soldiers
C) It is given to the killer's family
D) It is destroyed as unclean

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, salb is not the killer's property but is part of the collective booty (ghanimah) unless the imam designates it as nafal.

Detailed Scenarios of Salb Distribution and Textual Correction
Discusses various scenarios of salb distribution and corrects a textual error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on salb to various nuanced scenarios and identify the correct narration in al-Muhīt regarding the imam's offer.

Core Idea
Even in complex situations like when the polytheists take the salb and are defeated, the rule remains: salb is ghanimah for the army unless explicitly designated as nafal.

This lesson builds on the basic ruling to explore intricate scenarios: what if the salb was borrowed from a minor? What if it originally belonged to a Muslim? What if the polytheists capture and then lose it? These cases test the application of the principle that salb follows the ghanimah rules. Additionally, we examine a textual correction regarding the imam's statement about the horse.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle: salb is ghanimah
The killer has no automatic right; all salb goes to the collective booty unless the imam makes a nafal.
This underpins all scenarios.
2
Apply to borrowed items
If the salb was borrowed from a woman or child, it is still ghanimah because their property is treated like that of an adult.
The source says 'يستغنم مالهما كمال البالغ'.
3
Apply to items usurped from a Muslim
If the salb originally belonged to a Muslim who entered with aman and the polytheist usurped it, the polytheist owned it by domination, so it becomes ghanimah.
The Muslim's ownership ceases once the polytheist seizes it.
4
Apply to cases where polytheists take and are defeated
If the polytheists take the salb and then are defeated, it becomes ghanimah again. The killer loses his claim because the polytheists owned it temporarily.
Exception: if the salb was stripped from the killed, it is fay', not ghanimah.
5
Analyze the horse nafal scenario
If the imam says 'Whoever kills a horseman gets his horse,' a foot soldier who kills a foot soldier with a horse nearby gets the horse only if the horse is close in the battle line.
The imam's intent is to encourage killing those capable of fighting as horsemen.
6
Identify the textual correction
A commentator mistakenly cited 'gets his salb' from al-Muhīt, but the correct wording is 'gets his horse.' This is a scribal error noted by al-Ramlī.
The correction preserves consistency with the principle that salb is for all.
