ولو مسلمة ورثه الابن إن مات على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب وإن لحق المرتد بماله فظهر عليه فهو فيء فإن رجع وذهب بماله وظهر عليه فلوارثه وإن الحق وقضى بعبده ملكاً للورثة والوجه ظاهر الرواية كذا في فتح القدير تبعاً لما في النهاية والعناية وهما تبعا فخر الإسلام البزدوي في شرح الجامع الصغير من أن ظاهر الرواية الإطلاق. وقيد الفقيه أبو الليث في شرح الجامع الصغير بأن يكون الرجوع بعد القضاء أما قبله ففيء. وحمل في غاية البيان إطلاق الكتاب على مذهب محمد وما في بعض روايات السير على مذهب أبي يوسف. وبما قررناه سقط إشكال الزيلعي على النهاية لأنه حيث كان ظاهر الرواية الإطلاق وكان له وجه ظاهر فلا محل للإشكال فلذا قال في الفتح والوجه ظاهر الرواية واعتمده المصنف في الكافي قوله : ( وإن الحق وقضى بعبده لابنه فكاتبه فجاء مسلماً فالمكاتبة والولاء لمورثه) وهو المرتد الذي عاد مسلماً لأنه لا وجه إلى إبطال الكتابة لنفوذها بدليل منفذ وهو القضاء بلحاقه فجعلنا الوارث الذي هو خلفه كالوكيل من جهته وحقوق العقد فيه ترجع إلى الموكل والولاء لمن يقع العتق عنه نظيره المكاتب إذا كاتب عبده ثم عجز وفسخت الكتابة الأولى تبقى الثانية على حالها ويكون بدل الكتابة وولاؤه لمولاه، وليس انتقال الكتابة إلى المرتد الذي أسلم انتقال المكاتب من ملك الابن إليه وإنما هو لسقوط ولاية الخلف عند ظهور ولاية الأصل، وأشار بفاء التعقيب في قوله فجاء مسلماً إلى أن مجيئه عقيب كتابته يعني من غير أداء بدل الكتابة إلى الابن فلو أداها إليه ثم جاء مسلماً فإنه عتق على الابن حين أدى وكان الولاء له فلا ينتقل بعده إلى أبيه كما لو أعتق الابن عبده ثم جاء مسلماً والمكاتبة بدل الكتابة. وقيد بالكتابة لأن الابن إذا دبره ثم جاء الأب مسلماً فإن الولاء لا يكون للأب كما في التتارخانية. وأشار بكون البدل والولاء فقط للأب إلى أنه لا يمكن فسخ الكتابة لصدورها عن ولاية شرعية وقد صرح به الشارح وقدمنا عن الخانية أنه يملك إبطال كتابة الوارث قبل البدل إلا أن يقال إن مرادهم أنه لا يمكن فسخها بمجرد مجيئه من غير أن يفسخها، أما إذا فسخها انفسخت إلا أن جعلهم الوارث كالوكيل من جهته يأباه وقدمنا حكم ما إذا كاتب ثم ارتد ثم لحق . بسبب أداء. جميع
And if a female apostate, her son inherits from her if she dies on apostasy or joins dar al-harb. And if the apostate joins (the enemy) with his property and it appears (is captured), it is fay'. If he returns and takes his property and then appears (is captured), it goes to his heir. And if he joins and the judge rules his slave as owned by the heirs, the apparent (position) is unrestrictive, as in Fath al-Qadir following what is in al-Nihaya and al-Inaya, and both follow Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that the apparent (riwaya) is absolute. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth restricted it in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that the return must be after the ruling; before it, it is fay'. And in Ghayat al-Bayan, the absolute (text) is attributed to Muhammad's school, and some riwayat of al-Siyar to Abu Yusuf's school. By what we have established, the objection of al-Zayla'i on al-Nihaya is removed because since the apparent (riwaya) is absolute and has a clear basis, there is no room for objection. Hence, in al-Fath, he said: 'The apparent is the riwaya,' and the author in al-Kafi relied on it. His saying: (And if he joined and the judge ruled his slave as belonging to his son, then he (the son) made a kitabah contract with him, and then he (the apostate) came as a Muslim, then the kitabah and wala' are for his heir (the apostate father).) He is the apostate who returned as Muslim. Because there is no basis to invalidate the kitabah due to its validity by a valid proof, which is the judge's ruling upon his joining (dar al-harb). So we consider the heir, who is his successor, as a deputy from his side. The rights of the contract revert to the principal (the apostate), and the wala' belongs to the one by whom the emancipation is realized. Its analogy: a mukatib, if he makes a kitabah contract with his slave, then (the slave) becomes unable, and the first kitabah is rescinded, the second remains as it was, and the compensation and wala' are for his master. And the transfer of the kitabah to the apostate who became Muslim is not a transfer of the mukatib from the son's ownership; rather, it is due to the lapse of the successor's guardianship upon the emergence of the original guardian's guardianship. And he indicated by the letter fa (consequence) in his saying 'faja'a musliman' that his coming should be immediately after his (the son's) kitabah, meaning without the son having received the kitabah compensation. So if he (the son) had received it and then he (the apostate) came as a Muslim, then (the slave) becomes free upon the son at the time of payment, and wala' is for him (the son), so it does not transfer afterwards to his father, just as if the son had freed his slave and then the father came as a Muslim. And the kitabah is like (the case of) compensation. He restricted (the ruling) to kitabah because if the son had made a tadbir (declaration that the slave is free after his death) and then the father came as a Muslim, then wala' does not belong to the father, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And he indicated by saying that only the compensation and wala' are for the father that it is not possible to rescind the kitabah because it issued from a valid guardianship, and the commentator has stated it explicitly. And we have mentioned from al-Khaniyya that he has the right to invalidate the heir's kitabah before (payment of) the compensation. However, it may be said that their intent is that it cannot be rescinded merely by his (the father's) coming without him rescinding it; but if he rescinds it, it is rescinded, except that their making the heir as a deputy from his side precludes this. And we have mentioned the ruling when one makes a kitabah, then apostatizes, then joins (dar al-harb). Due to payment. All.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on an apostate's property and the effect of a kitabah contract made by his son. The first part states that if a female apostate dies or joins dar al-harb, her son inherits. If an apostate joins dar al-harb with his property and it is captured, it is fay' (spoils). If he returns, takes his property, and is then captured, it goes to his heir. The commentary clarifies the apparent (zahir) position of the school: the ruling is absolute; Abu al-Layth restricts it to return after the ruling of the judge, otherwise it is fay'. There is a difference in attribution: Ghayat al-Bayan attributes the absolute wording to Muhammad's opinion and some siyar narrations to Abu Yusuf. The main issue is when the apostate's son (the heir) makes a kitabah contract with the slave after the judge rules the slave belongs to the heir, and then the apostate returns as Muslim. The ruling: the kitabah and wala' belong to the apostate (the original owner), not to the son. The reasoning: the heir acts as a deputy; rights revert to the original owner. The condition is that the returning apostate comes before the son has received the kitabah compensation. If the son received it, the slave is freed by the son and wala' stays with him. The ruling is specific to kitabah; tadbir would not transfer wala' to the father. The text also discusses the possibility of rescinding the kitabah.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A contract of manumission by which a slave purchases his freedom in installments; the slave becomes a mukatib.
ولاء (walā’): The bond of clientage between a freed slave and his former master, giving the master the right to inherit from the freed slave in certain cases.
مرتد (murtadd): A person who apostatizes from Islam; during the state of riddah, their property and acts are subject to special rules.
فيء (fay’): Spoils taken from apostates or enemies without battle; becomes public treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the apostate returns before the judge's ruling.
B) Regardless of when he returns, as long as the son has not received the kitabah compensation.
C) Only if the apostate was a Muslim originally.
D) The contract never transfers; it always stays with the son.

Key Takeaway
When an apostate returns as Muslim, a kitabah contract made by his heir reverts to him, provided the heir has not yet received the compensation.

Diya for a Muslim Killed by an Apostate's Mistake: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diya payment source for an apostate's mistake killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya from an apostate's property and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the source of payment (kasb al-Islam vs. kasb al-ridda).

Core Idea
An apostate's diya for killing a Muslim by mistake is wajib, but from which earnings is the key dispute.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when an apostate kills a Muslim by mistake, the diya is wajib, but the source of payment depends on whether the apostate has earnings from his Muslim period (kasb al-Islam) or from his apostasy (kasb al-ridda). The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), and introduces an analogy to debt repayment. You will learn the positions, the proofs, and the mufta bihi (operative) view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an apostate killing a Muslim by mistake. The ruling is that diya is wajib (obligatory). Determine what constitutes the obligation.
2
Determine the source of payment according to the initial dispute
Note the disagreement: If the apostate has only kasb al-ridda, Abu Hanifa says the jinaaya is hader (invalid) – no diya. The Sahibayn say diya must be paid from both earnings if both exist, or from whichever exists if only one.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to debt
The text draws an analogy to debt (dayn) repayment. Abu Hanifa has three narrations on how debts are paid from kasb al-Islam and kasb al-ridda. Apply this analogy to the diya case.
The correct view according to al-Tatarkhaniyya is that diya is taken from kasb al-Islam first, then from kasb al-ridda.
4
Synthesize the mufta bihi position
Based on the three riwayat and later authorities, the practical ruling (mufta bihi) is to pay diya from kasb al-Islam first, and if insufficient, from kasb al-ridda.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل مرتد رجلاً خطأ ولحق أو قتل على الردة لأنه لو أسلم تكون الدية في الكسبين جميعاً مات أو لم يمت. وأشار بقوله «خاصة» إلى أنه لو لم يكن له كسب إسلام وإنما له كسب الردة فإن الجناية هدر عنده خلافاً لهما؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفيه نظر والصواب أن الدية في كسب الردة لأنها كالدين وقدمنا عن أبي حنيفة في الدين ثلاث روايات ; وفي رواية يقضى دين الإسلام من كسبه ودين الردة من كسبها، وفي رواية يقضى من كسب الردة إلا أن لا يفي فمن كسب الإسلام، وفي رواية عكسه وهي الصحيحة فلم يرد أن دين الردة هدر فكيف يقال في جنايته مع وجود كسب الردة أنها هدر والظاهر أنه سهو ولذا قال في التتارخانية والولوالجية : فإن لم يكن له إلا كسب الإسلام أو إلا كسب الردة تستوفى الدية منه، وإن كان له الكسبان قالا يستوفى منهما. وقال الإمام : تستوفى من كسب الإسلام أولاً فإن فضل شيء استوفى الفضل من كسب الردة اهـ.
If an apostate kills a man by mistake and joins the enemy or dies upon apostasy (because if he becomes Muslim, the diya would be from both earnings, whether he dies or not). He (the author) indicated by his saying 'especially' that if he has no Islamic earnings but only earnings from apostasy, then the crime is unpunishable in his (Abu Hanifa's) view, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Thus in Fath al-Qadir. There is consideration: the correct view is that the diya is from the earnings of apostasy because it is like a debt. We have mentioned from Abu Hanifa three narrations regarding debt: in one narration, the debt of Islam is paid from his Islamic earnings and the debt of apostasy from its earnings; in another narration, it is paid from the earnings of apostasy unless it does not suffice, then from Islamic earnings; in another narration, the opposite, and that is the correct one. So it is not said that the debt of apostasy is invalid, so how can it be said that his crime despite the existence of apostasy earnings is invalid? Apparently this is an oversight. Hence it is stated in al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Walwaljiyya: if he has only Islamic earnings or only apostasy earnings, the diya is taken from it; if he has both earnings, the two (Sahibayn) say it is taken from both, and Imam (Abu Hanifa) says: it is taken from Islamic earnings first, then if something remains, it is taken from apostasy earnings. End.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) on the diya (blood money) owed by an apostate (murtadd) who kills a Muslim by mistake (qatl khata). The ruling is part of the chapter on apostates from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a Hanafi commentary. The author discusses the source from which the diya should be paid when the apostate has both earnings from his Muslim period (kasb al-Islam) and earnings from his apostasy period (kasb al-ridda). According to Abu Hanifa, if the apostate has only earnings from apostasy, the diya is not due (hader), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) disagree. The passage then draws an analogy to debts (dayn) and presents three narrations from Abu Hanifa on how debts are paid from these earnings, concluding that the correct view is that the diya is taken from kasb al-Islam first. The final resolution from al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Walwaljiyya is that if only one type of earnings exists, it suffices; if both exist, the Sahibayn say from both, while Abu Hanifa prioritizes Islamic earnings. This shows the Hanafi internal ikhtilaf (intra-madhhab dispute) and the preferred (mufta bihi) position.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam (apostate).
كسب الإسلام (kasb al-Islam): Earnings acquired during the period when the person was Muslim.
دية (diya): Blood money compensation for homicide or injury.
كسب الردة (kasb al-ridda): Earnings acquired after apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From kasb al-ridda only
B) From both equally
C) From kasb al-Islam first, then from kasb al-ridda
D) From kasb al-ridda first, then from kasb al-Islam

Key Takeaway
An apostate's diya for mistakenly killing a Muslim is wajib, and the mufta bihi Hanafi position pays it first from kasb al-Islam, then from kasb al-ridda.

Is a Minor's Islam Valid and Does He Need a New Intention at Puberty?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on minor's Islam and intention at puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a minor's Islam and explain why intention for faith is not required upon puberty.

Core Idea
A minor's Islam is valid and no new intention for faith is required at puberty because the obligation is established by the cause, not by the address.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a subtle but important issue: is a child's conversion valid? And when he grows up, does he need to make a fresh intention to believe? The author presents the Hanafi majority view, supported by two leading scholars, and notes an opposing opinion. Understanding this issue clarifies the nature of obligation in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What are we asking? Is a minor's declaration of faith effective? Does it need renewal at puberty?
2
Understand Fakhr al-Islam's view
The cause (being an inhabitant of the world with reason) establishes the basis of obligation, but performance is not required until the address reaches the minor.
Al-Bazdawi: obligation exists potentially from cause.
3
Understand Shams al-A'imma's view
No obligation at all for the minor; his act is like a traveler praying Jumu'ah—the obligation drops but the act is valid.
Al-Sarakhsi: no ruling of obligation of performance.
4
Understand Maturidi's view
The sane minor is directly addressed with the obligation of faith like an adult.
Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: if minor dies without faith, he is eternally in Fire.
5
Determine the majority view
The majority (Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) agree that no new intention is required. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The author implies agreement with the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن والإقرار غير المنوي به إسقاط الفرض كما أنه لو كان يواظب على الصلاة قبل بلوغه لا يكون كما كان يفعله بل لا يكفيه بعد بلوغه منها إلا ما قرنه بنية أداء الواجب امتثالاً لكنهم اتفقوا على أنه لا يجب بل يقع فرضاً قبل البلوغ، أما عند فخر الإسلام فلأنه يثبت أصل الوجوب على الصبي بالسبب وهو حدث العالم وعقلية دلالته دون وجوب الأداء لأنه بالخطاب وهو غير مخاطب، فإذا وجد بعد السبب وقع الفرض كتعجيل الزكاة وأما عند شمس الأئمة لا أصلاً لعدم حكمه وهو وجوب الأداء فإذا وجد وجد كالمسافر يصلي الجمعة فيسقط وجوب فرضه وليست الجمعة فرضاً عليه لكن ذلك للترفية عليه بعد سببها فإذا فعلها تم، ولا نعلم خلافاً بين المسلمين في عدم وجوب نية فرض الإيمان بعد البلوغ على قول من حكم بصحة إسلامه صبياً تبعاً لأبويه المسلمين أو لإسلامه وأبواه كافران ولو كان ذلك فرضاً لم ينقله أهل الإجماع عن آخرهم .اهـ. ولم يذكر القول الثالث المختار عند أبي منصور الماتريدي وهو الصبي العاقل مخاطب بأداء الإيمان كالبالغ حتى لو مات بعده بلا إيمان خلد في النار، وذكره في التجريد.
That the declaration (of faith) not intended to drop an obligation, just as if he used to persist in prayer before his puberty, it is not as he used to do, but rather what suffices him after his puberty from it is only that which he accompanies with the intention of performing the obligatory duty in compliance. But they agreed that it is not obligatory, rather it occurs as an obligation before puberty. According to Fakhr al-Islam, because the basis of obligation is established upon the minor by the cause, which is the event of the world and the indication of reason, without the obligation of performance because it is by address, and he is not addressed. So when it occurs after the cause, the obligation occurs, like advancing zakat. And according to Shams al-A'imma, not at all because there is no ruling of obligation of performance, so when it occurs, it occurs like a traveler praying Friday prayer: the obligation of his obligatory prayer drops, and Friday is not obligatory upon him, but that is for alleviation after its cause, so when he performs it, it is complete. And we do not know of any disagreement among the Muslims regarding the non-obligation of the intention of the obligation of faith after puberty, according to the one who ruled on the validity of his Islam as a minor, following his Muslim parents, or because of his own Islam while his parents were disbelievers. And if that were obligatory, the people of consensus would have transmitted it from their last ones. End. And he did not mention the third opinion, the preferred one according to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, which is that the sane minor is addressed with the performance of faith like an adult, so that if he dies after it without faith, he will be eternally in the Fire, and it is mentioned in al-Tajrid.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the validity and obligation of a minor's Islam. The author, Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, explains that although a minor's acts of worship do not become obligatory until puberty—because he is not addressed by the command—his Islam is valid and does not require a new intention of faith upon reaching adulthood. He presents the reasoning of Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) and Shams al-A'imma (al-Sarakhsi). Fakhr al-Islam argues that the cause of obligation (being an inhabitant of the world with reason) establishes the basis of obligation, but performance is not required until the address reaches the minor. Shams al-A'imma goes further, holding that no obligation exists at all for the minor. Both agree that intention for faith is not required upon puberty. The author notes that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi had a third view: the sane minor is directly addressed with the obligation of faith like an adult, but this is omitted here and found in al-Tajrid.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): The verbal declaration of faith (shahada).
نية (niyyah): Intention required for an act of worship to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, does a minor who reached puberty need to make a new intention for faith?
A) Yes, because faith becomes obligatory only at puberty.
B) No, because his Islam as a minor is valid and sufficient.
C) Yes, according to Shams al-A'imma.
D) No, only if his parents were Muslims.

Key Takeaway
A minor's Islam is valid and no new intention for faith is required upon puberty; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Faction Condition for Harsh Measures Against Rebels
Ruling on faction condition for harsh measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for killing rebels' wounded and pursuing fugitives, and explain why the existence of a faction (fī'a) changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Killing wounded and pursuing fugitives is only allowed when rebels have an organized faction.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key conditional ruling in the Hanafi law of rebellion (baghy). The imam's permission to take harsher measures depends on whether the rebels possess a distinct faction capable of regrouping. Understanding this condition is essential for distinguishing between legitimate military necessity and unlawful force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and condition
Read the opening sentence: if rebels have a faction (fī'a), then kill wounded and follow fugitives; otherwise not. Note the direct contrast between the two scenarios.
The ruling is conditional on the presence of a faction.
2
Understand the rationale
The text explains: if they have a faction, pursuing them is necessary to repel their evil because they can regroup. Without a faction, the threat is minimized and such measures are not needed.
The rationale is repelling evil (daf' al-sharr).
3
Define fī'a
The text defines fī'a as a group (ta'ifa). Recognize that the definition is key—without a cohesive group, the harsh measures do not apply.
Plurals: fu'oon and fiat.
4
Consider the captive ruling
The passage adds that the imam may kill or imprison captives at his discretion, as per al-Bada'i'. This shows the imam's authority to choose the appropriate measure.
This is linked to the overall leniency toward rebels.
5
Note the evidentiary basis
The Hanafi position is grounded in the practice of 'Ali (RA) and the principle that rebels are not treated like enemy combatants.
The proof is a qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبدأ بقتالهم ولو لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم واتبع موليهم وإلا لا ولم تسب ذريتهم برهونهم فيجبرون على الإسلام أو يصيروا ذمة. وفي الهداية : وإذا بلغه أنهم يشترون السلاح ويتأهبون للقتال ينبغي أن يأخذهم ويحبسهم حتى يقلعوا عن ذلك ويحدثوا توبة دفعاً للشر بقدر الإمكان .
قوله : (ولو لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم واتبع موليهم وإلا (لا) أي وإن لم يكن لهم فئة لا يجهز على الجريح ولا يتبع المولى لدفع شرهم بالأول كيلا يلحقوا بهم ولاندفاع الشر دونه في الثاني، والفئة الطائفة والجمع فئون وفئات وجهز على الجريح كمنع وأجهز ثبت قتله وأسرعه وتمم عليه وموت مجهز وجهيز سريع؛ كذا في القاموس. «واتبع» على البناء للمفعول للقتل والأسر وموليهم بالنصب مفعول ثانٍ وهو اسم فاعل من ولي تولية أدبر كتولى ولم يذكر حكم أسيرهم. وفي البدائع : إن شاء الإمام قتله وإن شاء حبسه لاندفاع شره به. ويقاتل أهل البغي بالمنجنيق والغرق وغير ذلك كأهل الحرب، ب، وكل من لا يجوز قتله من أهل الحرب من النساء والصبيان والشيوخ والعميان لا يجوز قتله من أهل البغي إلا إذا قاتلوا فيقتلون حال القتال وبعد الفراغ إلا الصبيان والمجانين. ولا يجوز للعادل أن يبتدئ بقتل محرمه من أهل البغي مباشرة إلا إذا أراد قتله فله أن يدفعه ولو بقتله، وله أن يتسبب ليقتله غيره كعقر دابته بخلاف أهل الحرب فإن له أن يقتل محرمه منهم مباشرة إلا الوالدين اهـ.
He began with their fighting. If they have a faction, his wounded are killed and his fugitive is followed, otherwise not. And their offspring are not taken captive, their property is held until they repent, and they are compelled to Islam or become dhimmis. In Al-Hidayah: When he learns that they are buying weapons and preparing for battle, he should seize them and imprison them until they desist and renew repentance, to repel evil as much as possible. His statement: 'If they have a faction, his wounded are killed and his fugitive is followed; otherwise not' — that is, if they have no faction, the wounded is not killed nor the fugitive followed, to repel their evil by the first, lest they join them, and the evil is not repelled by the second. 'Faction' is a group; its plurals are fu'oon and fiat. 'Jahhaza 'ala al-jarih' like 'mana'a' and 'ajhaza' means to kill him quickly, complete him; 'majhaz' and 'jihiz' mean quick. So in Al-Qamus. 'Uttubi'a' is passive for killing and capture, 'mawlihim' is accusative as second object, the active participle from 'tawalla' meaning to turn his back. He did not mention the ruling for their captive. In Al-Bada'i': If the imam wishes, he may kill him, or if he wishes, he may imprison him to repel his evil. The people of transgression are fought with catapults, drowning, and other means like the people of war. And anyone from the people of war whose killing is not permitted — women, boys, elderly, blind — their killing from the people of transgression is not permitted unless they fight; then they are killed during the fighting and after cessation, except boys and madmen. It is not permissible for the just person to initiate the killing of his mahram from the people of transgression directly unless he intends to kill him; then he may repel him even by killing him, and he may cause another to kill him, like hamstringing his beast, unlike the people of war where he may kill his mahram among them directly except parents.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage clarifies the rules of engagement with rebels (bughat). The text distinguishes between two scenarios: when rebels have a faction (fī'a) and when they do not. Having a faction implies organized resistance and a stronghold. In that case, the imam may kill the wounded among them and pursue those who flee. This is because the evil is not fully repelled otherwise, as they might regroup. When they have no faction, such measures are unnecessary as the threat is minimal. The passage also mentions that the offspring of rebels are not taken captive; instead, their property is held until they repent. The imam has discretion to kill or imprison captives. The rules for who may be killed among rebels mirror those for war: non-combatants are protected unless they fight. Special rules apply to mahrams (close relatives) of the just fighter: he may not initiate killing them directly but may defend himself.

Key Terms
فئة (fī'a): A faction or organized group of rebels that provides strength and a support base.
بغي (baghy): Rebellion or transgression against the legitimate imam; the rebels are called bughat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when may the imam kill the wounded of the rebels?
A) Always, because they are rebels
B) Only if they have a faction (fī'a)
C) Only if they have no faction
D) Never, because rebels are protected

Key Takeaway
Killing wounded rebels and pursuing fugitives is permissible only when the rebels have an organized faction; otherwise, such measures are not allowed.

Permissible Fighting Methods and Protection of Non-Combatants Among Rebels
Rules on fighting methods and non-combatant immunity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which non-combatants are protected from killing when fighting rebels and explain the special rules for mahrams.

Core Idea
Non-combatants among rebels are protected unless they fight; mahrams require extra caution.

This passage continues the rules of engagement with rebels. After establishing the faction condition, the text now addresses the methods of fighting and who may be killed. The Hanafi school balances military necessity with protection of non-combatants, even in rebellion.

Guided Path
1
Identify permissible fighting methods
The text states that rebels may be fought with catapults, drowning, and other means, just like enemy combatants. This indicates that the imam may use necessary force.
No special restrictions on methods beyond those for regular war.
2
List protected non-combatants
Women, boys (sibyan), elderly (shuyukh), and blind (umyan) are normally not killed. They are protected as long as they do not participate in fighting.
This mirrors the rules for enemy combatants (ahl al-harb).
3
Apply the exception for fighters
If these protected categories actually fight, they lose protection and may be killed during the fighting. However, after the fighting ceases, only boys and madmen are spared; others may still be killed if they fought.
The exception shows that active participation changes the ruling.
4
Understand the mahram rule
A just fighter (al-adil) may not initiate killing his mahram (close relative) from the rebels. But if the mahram intends to kill him, he may defend himself even by killing. He may also cause the mahram's death indirectly, e.g., by hamstringing his mount.
This is more restrictive than for enemy combatants.
5
Contrast with enemy combatants
For enemy combatants (ahl al-harb), one may kill his mahram directly except parents. The leniency for rebels is due to their status as misguided Muslims.
The difference highlights the unique treatment of bughat.

Source Text

Key Terms

مجانين (majanin): Insane persons; considered non-culpable and thus protected even if they fight.

Treatment of Rebels' Property and Spoils
Rulings on holding property, not taking captives, and using rebels' weapons when needed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rebels' property, cite the proof from 'Ali (RA), and apply the rule in distinguishing between types of property.

Core Idea
Rebels' property is held in trust, not taken as spoils; horses sold, price held.

This lesson examines the Hanafi treatment of rebels' property during conflict. Based on the precedent of Caliph 'Ali (RA) at the Battle of the Camel, the school holds that rebels' property is not spoils of war but is held until repentance, with specific rules for horses and weapons. Understanding this distinction is crucial for differentiating between legitimate combatants and internal insurgents in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal obligation regarding rebels' property? The Hanafi ruling is that it is wajib to hold their property until they repent.
The hukm is not spoils (ghanima) but a trust (amānah).
2
Locate the dalil
What is the proof cited? The practice of 'Ali (RA) at the Battle of the Camel: 'No prisoner is killed, no curtain is uncovered, no property is taken.'
This is a qawl al-ṣaḥābī, considered a proof in Hanafi uṣūl.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof yield the ruling? The explicit prohibition of taking property indicates that rebels' property is not lawful spoils.
The words 'lā yuʾkhadhu māl' are general and clear.
4
Note intra-school details
What are specific sub-rulings? Slaves who fought may be killed; others are imprisoned. Horses are sold and the price held. Weapons may be used if needed.
These details expand the general ruling.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference from other schools? Shafi'is may treat rebels' property as spoils; Hanafis hold it in trust, affecting distribution and return.
This difference has significant implications for post-conflict reconciliation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم تسب ذريتهم وحبس أموالهم حتى يتوبوا القول علي رضي الله عنه يوم الجمل : ولا يقتل أسير ولا يكشف ستر ولا يؤخذ مال وهو القدوة في هذا الباب. وقوله في لا الأسير مؤول بما إذا لم يكن لهم فئة . ومعنى لا يكشف لهم ستر تسبى نساؤهم. أطلق المال فشمل العبيد فلذا قال في البدائع : وأما العبد المأسور من أهل البغي فإن كان قاتل مع مولاه يجوز قتله، وإن كان يخدم مولاه لا يجوز قتله ولكن يحبس حتى يتوب اهـ. وظاهر ما الكتاب حبس عين الكراع وليس كذلك لما في الهداية وأما الكراع فلا يمسك ولكنه يباع ويحبس ثمنه لمالكه لأنه أنفع له وذكر في المحيط الدواب بدل الكراع . وفي فتح القدير : ولا ينفق عليه من بيت المال لتتوفر مؤنتها عليه وهذا إذا لم يكن للإمام بها حاجة اهـ. قوله : (وإن احتاج قاتل بسلاحهم وخيلهم لأن علياً رضي الله عنه قسم السلاح فيما بين أصحابه بالبصرة وكانت قسمته للحاجة لا للتمليك، ولأن للإمام أن يفعل ذلك في مال العادل عند الحاجة ففي مال الباغي أولى والمعنى فيه إلحاق الضرر الأدنى لدفع الأعلى. قيد بالسلاح والخيل لأن غيرهما من الأموال لا ينتفع به مطلقاً؛ كذا في البدائع.
And their offspring are not taken captive, and their property is held until they repent, following the statement of 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, on the Day of the Camel: 'No prisoner is killed, no curtain is uncovered, no property is taken.' He is the exemplar in this chapter. His statement about the prisoner is interpreted as when they have no faction. The meaning of 'no curtain is uncovered for them' is that their women are not taken captive. The word 'property' is general, including slaves. Hence in Al-Bada'i': As for the captured slave from the people of transgression: if he fought with his master, his killing is permissible; if he served his master, his killing is not permissible, but he is imprisoned until he repents. The apparent meaning of the book is the detention of the actual horse (kurā'), but that is not the case according to Al-Hidayah: the horse is not held, but it is sold and its price is held for its owner, because that is more beneficial for him. In Al-Muhit, 'animals' are mentioned instead of 'horses'. In Fath al-Qadir: Nothing is spent on them from the treasury to burden them, and this applies when the imam has no need for them. His statement: 'If there is need, they fight with their weapons and horses' because 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, divided the weapons among his companions in Basra, and his division was for need, not for ownership. And because the imam may do that with the property of the just person in need, so with the property of the transgressor it is more apt. The meaning is to inflict the lesser harm to repel the greater. It is restricted to weapons and horses because other property is not utilized absolutely; as in Al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This section focuses on the treatment of rebels' property. Based on the example of Caliph 'Ali (RA), the Hanafi position is that rebels' property is not taken as spoils of war (ghanima) but is held in trust until they repent. Slaves captured from rebels may be killed only if they actively fought; otherwise they are imprisoned. Horses and weapons of rebels are not simply confiscated: they may be sold and the proceeds held for the owner, unless the imam has need, in which case he may use them. The principle is to inflict the lesser harm to repel the greater evil. This distinguishes rebels from enemy combatants, whose property is spoils.

Key Terms
الكراع (al-kurā'): Horses or equestrian equipment used in battle.
حبس مال (habs al-māl): Holding property in trust without distributing it, until the owner repents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is taken as spoils and distributed
B) It is kept by the imam permanently
C) It is sold and the price held for the owner
D) It is destroyed

Key Takeaway
Rebels' property is not spoils; it is held in trust, with specific rules for horses and weapons to balance justice and need.

Repentance of Rebels and Its Effect
Conditions for accepting a rebel's repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for accepting a rebel's repentance and apply them to distinguish valid from invalid repentance.

Core Idea
A rebel's repentance requires both verbal confession and laying down arms to become inviolable.

This passage from Al-Muhit clarifies the precise conditions under which a rebel (baghi) is granted inviolability. The ruling hinges on the sincerity of repentance, demonstrated through both speech and action. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the law of rebellion (ahkam al-bughat) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary requirement
The text states that a rebel saying 'I have repented' and laying down his weapon stops prosecution. This shows two conditions: verbal declaration and physical surrender.
2
Examine the conditional case
If the rebel asks for time to consider, he is still protected temporarily. This indicates that the condition for immediate protection is not instantaneous; seeking deliberation is allowed.
Protection is given even when the rebel is still deliberating.
3
Distinguish invalid claims
A mere statement 'I am on your religion' while armed does not count as repentance. Why? Because it lacks the surrender of arms, which is the physical manifestation of abandoning rebellion.
Verbal acceptance of the ruler's authority without disarming is insufficient.
4
Understand the analogy
The text compares the rebel's repentance to a combatant's conversion to Islam: both confer inviolability upon a change of status.
Just as a combatant becomes protected upon embracing Islam, a rebel becomes protected upon sincere repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : قال الباغي تبت وألقى السلاح كف عنه لأن توبة الباغي بمنزلة الإسلام من الحربي في إفادة العصمة والحرمة، ولو قال كف عني لأنظر في أمري لعلي ألقى السلاح يكف عنه، ولو قال أنا على دينك ومعه السلاح لم يكف عنه لأن ذلك ليس بتوبة .اهـ.
In Al-Muhit: If the rebel says, 'I have repented' and lays down his weapon, he is desisted from, because the repentance of the rebel is like the Islam of a combatant in conferring inviolability and sanctity. If he says, 'Stop me, let me consider my affair, perhaps I will lay down the weapon,' he is desisted from. But if he says, 'I am on your religion' while having the weapon, he is not desisted from, because that is not repentance.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from Al-Muhit clarifies when rebels should be given protection. True repentance involves a verbal declaration and laying down arms. Mere consideration or a verbal claim while still armed does not constitute repentance. The analogy to a combatant accepting Islam is used: just as a combatant becomes inviolable upon conversion, a rebel becomes inviolable upon sincere repentance.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba): Repentance consisting of verbal confession and abandoning rebellion by laying down weapons.
باغي (baghi): Rebel, one who fights against the legitimate ruler without justification.
عصمة (ismah): Inviolability, protection from being killed or punished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Muhit, what is the ruling when a rebel says 'I repent' and lays down his weapon?
A) He is still fought until he proves sincerity.
B) He is desisted from.
C) He is given a three-day grace period.
D) He is executed anyway.

Key Takeaway
A rebel's repentance is accepted only when both verbal declaration and laying down arms are present, granting inviolability.

Liability and Inheritance in Killings Between Just and Rebel
Rulings on liability, inheritance, and treatment of dead when just and rebel kill each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on liability, inheritance, and treatment of the dead when killings occur between just persons and rebels, and apply the conditions that affect these rulings.

Core Idea
Killing rebels in combat is lawful; inheritance is valid when the killer is just, and the imam's authority determines liability.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of rulings on killings between the just (supporters of the legitimate imam) and rebels. It covers when qisas applies, how the dead are treated, and whether inheritance is affected. Understanding these rules requires careful attention to the imam's authority and the combat context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different scenarios
There are three scenarios: rebel kills rebel, just kills rebel, and rebel kills just. Each has a different ruling based on the imam's authority and the context of combat.
2
Apply liability rulings
If a rebel kills another rebel, no liability because the imam's authority is absent. If a just person kills a rebel during combat, no liability. If a rebel kills a just person, qisas may apply under two conditions: intentional killing and the imam's authority not having been severed by the rebels applying their own laws.
The condition for qisas is that the imam's authority was still intact at the time of killing.
3
Determine treatment of the dead
The dead of the just are treated as martyrs. The dead of rebels are washed, shrouded, and buried, but no funeral prayer is offered. Taking heads and sending them away is disliked, though some later scholars permitted it for strategic benefit.
4
Apply inheritance rules
A just person who kills a rebel inherits from him because the killing was lawful. If a rebel kills a just person, inheritance depends on the rebel's claim: if he admits to being on falsehood, he does not inherit; if he claims to be on the truth, he inherits.
The ruling on inheritance is based on the lawfulness of the killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قتل باغ مثله فظهر عليهم لم يجب شيء لأنه لا ولاية لإمام العدل حين القتل فلم ينعقد موجباً كالقتل في دار الحرب فلا قصاص ولا دية ولذا عبر بالشيء المنكر في النفي، فظاهره أنه لا يأثم أيضاً وهو ظاهر ما في فتح القدير فإنه علل بأنه قتل نفساً يباح قتلها ألا ترى أن العادل إذا قتله لا يجب عليه شيء فلما كان مباح القتل لم يجب به شيء .اهـ. وفي البدائع : يصنع بقتلى أهل العدل ما يصنع بسائر الشهداء لأنهم شهداء، وأما قتلى أهل البغي فلا يصلى عليهم ولكنهم يغسلون ويكفنون ويدفنون، ويكره أن تؤخذ رؤوسهم وتبعث إلى الآفاق وكذلك رؤوس أهل الحرب لأنه مثلة اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وجوزه بعض المتأخرين إذا كان فيه طمأنينة قلوب أهل العدل أو كسر شوكتهم .اهـ. ومنعه في المحيط في رؤوس البغاة وجوزه في رؤوس أهل الحرب.
قوله : (وإن غلبوا على مصر فقتل مصري مثله فظهر على المصر قتل به يعني بشرطين : الأول إن كان عمداً الثاني أن لا يجري على أهله أحكام أهل البغي وأزعجوا من المصر قبل ذلك لأنه حينئذ لم تنقطع ولاية الإمام وبعد إجراء أحكامهم تنقطع فلا يجب. قوله: (وإن قتل عادل باغياً أو قتله باغ وقال أنا على حق ورثه وإن قال أنا على باطل لا) أي لا يرثه بيان لمسألتين: الأولى إذا قتل عادل باغياً فإنه يرثه ولا تفصيل فيه لأنه قتل بحق فلا يمنع الإرث، وأصله أن العادل إذا أتلف نفس الباغي أو ماله لا يضمن ولا يأثم لأنه مأمور بقتالهم دفعاً كذا لشرهم ؛ في الهداية. وصرح في البدائع بأن العادل لا يضمن ما أصاب من أهل البغي من دم أو جراحة أو مال استهلكه وفي شرح المختار قال محمد: إذا تابوا أفتيهم أن يغرموا ولا أجبرهم. وفي المحيط العادل لو أتلف مال الباغي يؤخذ بالضمان لأن مال الباغي معصوم في حقنا وأمكن إلزام الضمان له فكان في إيجابه فائدة ووفق الشارح فحمل عدم الضمان على ما إذا أتلفه حال القتال بسبب القتال إذ لا يمكنه أن يقتلهم إلا بإتلاف
His statement: 'If a rebel kills someone like him (another rebel) and they are later subdued, nothing is incumbent upon him because the authority of the just imam at the time of killing is not established, so no obligation arises, like killing in the abode of war, so there is no retaliation and no blood money. Hence he expressed with 'something' in the negation, so apparently he is not sinful either. That is apparent in Fath al-Qadir, for he reasoned that it is the killing of a life whose killing is permissible. Do you not see that if a just person kills him, nothing is incumbent upon him? Since killing is permissible, nothing is due. In Al-Bada'i': The dead of the just people are treated like other martyrs because they are martyrs. As for the dead of the rebels, no funeral prayer is offered over them, but they are washed, shrouded, and buried. It is disliked to take their heads and send them to regions, as well as the heads of people of war, because it is mutilation. In Fath al-Qadir: Some later scholars permitted it if it brings reassurance to the hearts of the just or breaks their might. Al-Muhit prohibits it for heads of rebels but permits it for heads of people of war. His statement: 'If they gain control of a city and a just person is killed, then they are subdued over the city, he is killed by it' means with two conditions: first that it was intentional, second that the rulings of the people of transgression had not been applied to its inhabitants and they had been expelled from the city before that; because then the authority of the imam had not ceased, but after the application of their rulings it ceases, so it is not obligatory. His statement: 'If a just person kills a rebel, or a rebel kills him and says: I am on the truth, he inherits from him; but if he says: I am on falsehood, he does not inherit' — that is, he does not inherit. This explains two issues: first, if a just person kills a rebel, he inherits from him with no detail because he killed by right, so inheritance is not prevented. The origin is that when a just person destroys the life or property of a rebel, he is not liable and does not sin because he is commanded to fight them to repel their evil. In Al-Hidayah, it is stated explicitly in Al-Bada'i' that the just person is not liable for any blood, wound, or property he consumes from the people of transgression. In Sharh al-Mukhtar, Muhammad said: 'When they repent, I give a legal opinion that they must compensate, but I do not force them.' In Al-Muhit: If a just person destroys the property of a rebel, he is held liable for compensation because the property of the rebel is inviolable in our regard and it is possible to impose compensation on him; there is benefit in requiring it. The commentator reconciled by restricting non-liability to cases of destruction during combat due to combat, since he cannot kill them except by destruction.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage deals with complex liability issues. When a rebel kills another rebel (a like person), no retaliation or blood money is due because the killing occurs in a state where the just imam's authority is not fully recognized. However, if rebels take over a city and kill a just person, and later the imam recovers the city, the killer may face qisas (retaliation) under two conditions: the killing was intentional, and before the rebels applied their own laws to the city, the imam's authority had not completely ceased. Regarding inheritance: if a just person kills a rebel, he inherits from him because the killing was lawful. Conversely, if a rebel kills a just person, and the rebel admits being on falsehood, he does not inherit. A rebel's property is considered inviolable in the sense that if a just person destroys it outside combat, he may be liable; during combat, however, liability is waived. There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars about whether rebels should compensate for damages after repentance; Muhammad al-Shaybani opined that they should compensate in principle but not be forced legally.

Key Terms
ولاية الإمام (wilāyat al-imām): Authority of the legitimate imam, which may be negated when rebels control a region.
باغ (bāghī): A rebel who fights against the legitimate imam with a misinterpretation.
إرث (irth): Inheritance; lawful killing does not bar inheritance in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a just person kills a rebel during combat, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Qisas is required
B) Diyah is required
C) No liability, no sin, and inheritance is valid
D) The just person is sinful but no financial penalty

Key Takeaway
The imam's authority at the time of killing determines liability; killing rebels in combat is lawful and incurs no penalty.

Congregational Mosque in Private Property: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mosque status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when a mosque in a private house or dead-end alley is considered a congregational mosque and identify the condition for i'tikaf validity.

Core Idea
A mosque in private property remains a congregational mosque if access is not permanently restricted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical question: When does a mosque located in a private house or a dead-end alley count as a congregational mosque? The ruling affects the validity of i'tikaf and other mosque-related rules. We will examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement and the reasoning (ta'lil) that leads to the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine what is being debated – the status of a mosque in a private house or non-through alley as a congregational mosque (masjid jama'a).
The consequence includes validity of i'tikaf.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf argues that if the door is closed during prayer but others can enter at other times, it is a congregational mosque, analogous to a mosque in a non-through alley.
The key analogy (qiyas) is with the non-through alley mosque.
3
Present Muhammad al-Azjandi's objection
Al-Azjandi requires a physical connection to a public way (a wall to a through street) for i'tikaf to be valid, because the alley's way is owned by residents.
This is a stricter condition focusing on the nature of the pathway.
4
Analyze the tarjih
The author (Ibn al-Humam) favors the view transmitted by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, which supports Abu Yusuf, citing actual practice in Bukhara and Khwarazm.
The author uses practical consensus as evidence.
5
Observe the practical application
In schools and fortresses where people are not prevented from praying, they are treated as congregational mosques.
This shows the operative ruling is based on ease and common practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اختلف في مسجد الدار والخان والرباط أنه مسجد جماعة أم لا والأصح ما روي عن أبي يوسف أنه إذا أغلق باب الدار فهو مسجد جماعة للجماعة التي في الدار إذا لم يمنعوا غيرهم من الصلاة فيه في سائر الأوقات لأن مسجد الزقاق الذي ليس بنافذ مسجد جماعة فإن صلوا فيه في وقت أغلقوا باب الزقاق كذا هذا وعنه إن كان فيه جماعة ممن في الدار بعد الإغلاق لا يمنعون غيرهم في الأوقات الأخر فهو مسجد جماعة وإلا فلا. (فخ) مثله . وعن محمد الأوزجندي: لا يجوز الاعتكاف في مسجد زقاق غير نافذ لأن طريقه مملوك لأهله إلا إذا كان له حائط إلى طريق نافذ فحينئذ يمكن التطرق إليه من حق العامة فيخلص الله تعالى فيصير مسجداً. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : والذي اختاره (فخ) أصح. وقد رأينا ببخارى وغيرها في دور وسكك في أزقة غير نافذة من غير شك الأئمة والعوام في كونها مساجد فعلى هذا المساجد التي في المدارس بجرجانية خوارزم مساجد لأنهم لا يمنعون الناس من الصلاة فيها، وإذا أغلقت يكون فيها جماعة من أهلها .اهـ.
In al-Qunyah: There is disagreement regarding the mosque of a house, a caravanserai, and a fortress: is it a congregational mosque or not? The most correct is what is narrated from Abu Yusuf that if the door of the house is closed, it is a congregational mosque for the congregation in the house as long as they do not prevent others from praying in it at other times. For the mosque of a non-through alley is a congregational mosque; if they pray in it at a time, they close the alley door, similarly this. And from him: if there is a congregation among the people of the house after closure, and they do not prevent others at other times, then it is a congregational mosque; otherwise not. (Fakhr) like it. And from Muhammad al-Azjandi: It is not permissible to perform i'tikaf in a mosque in a non-through alley because its way is owned by its residents, unless it has a wall to a through way, then it is possible to reach it via the public right, so it becomes a mosque. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The one I have chosen (Fakhr) is more correct. And we have seen in Bukhara and elsewhere in houses and streets in non-through alleys, without doubt from the imams and commoners that they are mosques. Accordingly, the mosques that are in the schools of Jurjaniyya of Khwarazm are mosques because they do not prevent people from praying in them, and when closed, there is a congregation from its residents. End.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the legal definition of a congregational mosque (masjid jama'a) when it is located within private property. The key issue is whether the mosque retains its public status if access is controlled. Abu Yusuf's opinion distinguishes between the mosque in a house vs. a dead-end alley, ruling that if the door is closed but others can still enter during other times, it remains a congregational mosque. Muhammad al-Azjandi adds a condition for i'tikaf: the mosque must have a direct connection to a public way. The author (Ibn al-Humam) favors Fakhr's choice and supports it by observing actual practice in Bukhara, where such mosques are treated as valid by both scholars and commoners. The passage emphasizes that the defining characteristic is unrestricted access, not the physical location.

Key Terms
مسجد الزقاق (Masjid al-Zuqaq): A mosque located in a dead-end alley whose way is owned by residents.
مسجد الدار (Masjid al-Dar): A mosque built within a private house for the residents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, what must be true for a mosque in a private courtyard to be valid for i'tikaf?
A) The mosque must have a separate entrance from the public street.
B) Non-residents must be able to enter at times other than prayer.
C) The residents must always keep the door open.
D) The mosque must be built by the community.

Key Takeaway
A mosque in a private house or dead-end alley is still a congregational mosque if access is not permanently restricted, following Abu Yusuf's view.

The Founder's Right to Imamate and Adhan
Ruling on founder's priority in mosque appointments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mosque founder's priority in appointing imam and mu'adhdhin, and apply the distinction between the two roles regarding the appointee's moral character.

Core Idea
The founder has priority in appointments unless the community finds a clearly better candidate.

Mosques are often established by individuals who build them and dedicate them as waqf. This lesson explores the rights of such founders in appointing the imam and mu'adhdhin, based on the Hanafi position. You will learn the default rule, the exception, and the different treatment of imam versus mu'adhdhin when the appointee is sinful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The ruling addresses a conflict between the mosque founder and the local community regarding repairs, appointment of mu'adhdhin, or imam. Recognize the parties and the source of contention.
The founder (al-bani) is the one who built the mosque and established it as a waqf.
2
State the default rule
The text begins with 'al-ashah' (the most correct view): the founder has priority over the community. Understand that this is the preferred Hanafi position.
This is from al-Mujarrad transmission from Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify the exception
The rule is not absolute: 'unless the people want what is more beneficial' (except an yurid al-qawm ma huwa aslah). Know that the community can override if they propose a more qualified candidate.
This balances individual right with communal interest.
4
Distinguish imam from mu'adhdhin
The passage makes a distinction: for mu'adhdhin, the founder's choice prevails even if the appointee is sinful (fasiq), but for imam, the founder cannot impose a sinful person. Note the different treatment.
Possibly because imam's role involves leading prayer and moral guidance.
5
Recognize the hereditary right
The founder's priority extends to his son and clan. Understand that this right passes to descendants and relatives, giving them precedence over non-relatives.
This is consistent with the idea of the founder's special connection to the mosque.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنازع أهل المحلة والباني في عمارته أو نصب المؤذن أو الإمام فالأصح أن الباني أولى به إلا أن يريد القوم ما هو أصلح وقيل الباني بالمؤذن أولى وإن كان فاسقاً بخلاف الإمام، والباني أحق بالإمامة والأذان وولده من بعده وعشيرته أولى بذلك من غيرهم. وفي المجرد عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه أن المسجد ونصب الإمام والمؤذن إذا تأهل للإمامة اهـ.
If the people of the neighborhood and the founder dispute regarding its repair or the appointment of the mu'adhdhin or imam, the most correct is that the founder has priority, unless the people want what is more beneficial. And it is said that the founder is more entitled regarding the mu'adhdhin even if he is sinful, unlike the imam. And the founder is more entitled to the imamate and adhan, and his son after him, and his clan are more entitled to that than others. And in al-Mujarrad from Abu Hanifa: that the mosque and the appointment of the imam and mu'adhdhin, if he is qualified for the imamate. End.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This ruling addresses disputes between mosque founders and the local community. The preferred Hanafi position (al-ashah) gives the founder priority over the community in making appointments and decisions about the mosque's affairs, unless the community proposes a clearly better candidate. A distinction is made between the imam and the mu'adhdhin: for the mu'adhdhin, the founder's choice prevails even if he is sinful, but for the imam, the founder cannot impose a sinful person. The founder's right extends to his descendants and clan. This is based on the transmission (mujarrad) from Abu Hanifa, which considers the founder's role in establishing the mosque as giving him special authority.

Key Terms
الباني (al-Bani): The person who built the mosque and established it as a waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The community's choice prevails.
B) The founder's choice prevails despite sin.
C) A sinful mu'adhdhin is not allowed at all.
D) The matter is left to the qadi.

Key Takeaway
The mosque founder's priority in appointments is not absolute; it yields when the community proposes a clearly better candidate, and the imam must be of good character unlike the mu'adhdhin.

Temporal Condition Invalidates Mosque
States that declaring a place a mosque for a limited time does not make it a mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a time-limited designation of a place as a mosque does not create a valid mosque according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mosque requires perpetual dedication; a time-limited designation is ineffective.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a clear ruling: declaring a place a mosque for a limited period—such as one prayer or one month—does not legally transform it into a mosque. The commentator links this to the broader Hanafi principle that waqf (endowment) must be perpetual. Understanding this ruling reinforces the importance of perpetuity in Islamic endowments and helps distinguish valid mosque designations from temporary arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Read the matn: a person says 'pray in it in congregation for one prayer or two, for a day or a month.' The act is declaring a place as a mosque for a limited time.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is temporal limitation (tawqit): the designation is not permanent but restricted to a specific duration.
3
State the ruling
The ruling: 'it does not become a mosque.' The place does not acquire the legal status of a mosque.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The commentator suggests the ruling is based on the principle that temporal conditions invalidate a waqf (al-tawqit mubtil). A mosque is a type of waqf, and waqf requires perpetuity (ta'bid).
5
Connect to broader Hanafi rules
In Hanafi law, all waqf must be perpetual. Any condition limiting duration renders the waqf void. This distinguishes the Hanafi position from other schools that allow temporary waqf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لكن لو قال صلوا فيه جماعة صلاة أو صلاتين يوماً أو شهراً لا يكون مسجداً) قال الشيخ علاء الدين في شرح الملتقى : لعله مفرع على أن التوقيت مبطل
His saying: (But if he said, 'Pray in it in congregation for one prayer or two prayers, for a day or a month,' it does not become a mosque.) Sheikh Ala al-Din said in Sharh al-Multaqa: Perhaps it is based on the fact that temporal condition is invalidating.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This is a direct quotation from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) followed by a brief commentary. The matn states that if a person designates a place for congregational prayer for a specific limited period, it does not acquire the legal status of a mosque. The commentator, Sheikh Ala al-Din, suggests that this ruling is derived from the principle that temporal conditions (tawqit) invalidate a waqf, because a waqf requires continuity and perpetuity (ta'bid). Therefore, a time-bound declaration does not create a valid mosque.

Key Terms
التوقيت (al-Tawqit): The condition of limiting the duration of a waqf or designation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a time-limited declaration of a mosque not make it a valid mosque?
A) Because a mosque requires a building.
B) Because a waqf requires perpetuity.
C) Because the community did not agree.
D) Because prayer in congregation is not enough.

Key Takeaway
A mosque designation must be perpetual; any temporal condition renders the declaration void.

Leftover Mosque Candles and Donor Permission
Ruling on leftover candles and donor permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking leftover mosque candles and identify the condition of donor permission.

Core Idea
Leftover mosque candles cannot be taken without donor consent, even if custom allows.

This passage from al-Qunyah clarifies the limits of the imam's authority over partially burned donated items. The ruling hinges on explicit or implicit donor permission, with custom playing a role only when clearly established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is taking the remaining part of candles sent to the mosque. The default ruling is that it is not permissible (haraam) because it is someone else's property without permission.
"ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذ" indicates prohibition.
2
Recognize the condition for permissibility
Taking becomes permissible only with the donor's explicit permission or if local custom ('urf) clearly allows it. Custom is a valid basis only if it is explicit and widespread.
Custom does not override the default prohibition unless it is explicit.
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a donor sends candles for Taraweeh and only stubs remain, the imam must refrain from taking them home unless the donor previously indicated or local practice clearly permits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية من آخر الوقف : بعث شمعاً في شهر رمضان إلى مسجد فاحترق وبقي منه ثلثه أو دونه ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذ بغير إذن الدافع ولو كان العرف في ذلك صريح في ذلك فله ذلك اهـ. وفيها : وكرهوا إحداث الطاقات في المساجد روي ذلك عن ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه. قيم الجامع القديم أجر موضعاً تحت ظلة الباب لبعض الصكاكين لا يصح . لا يجوز إزالة الحائط التي بين المسجدين ليجعلهما واحداً إذا لم يكن فيه مصلحة ظاهرة، وكذا رفع صفته ويضمن القيم ما أنفق فيه من مال المسجد. بنى في فنائه في الرستاق دكاناً لأجل الصلاة يصلون فيه بجماعة كل وقت فله حكم المسجد ولا يوضع الجذع على جدار المسجد وإن كان من أوقافه اهـ. وفيها من الكراهية ولو كان إلى المسجد مدخل من دار موقوفة لا بأس للإمام أن يدخل هذا الباب لأنه أن روي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يدخل من حجرته إلى المسجد. له في المسجد موضع معين يواظب عليه وقد شغله غيره قال الأوزاعي له أن يزعجه وليس له ذلك عندنا ويكره تخصيص مكان في المسجد لنفسه لأنه يخل بالخشوع . لا حرمة لتراب المسجد إذا جمع وله حرمة إذا بسط .
In al-Qunyah from the end of the chapter on waqf: Someone sent candles in Ramadan to a mosque, then they burned and only a third or less remained; it is not permissible for the imam or mu'adhdhin to take it without the permission of the sender, even if the custom was clear in that; if so, they may take it. End. And in it: They disapproved of creating openings (windows) in mosques; that is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him. The custodian of the old congregational mosque rented a spot under the canopy of the door to some knife sharpeners; it is not valid. It is not permissible to remove the wall between two mosques to make them one if there is no apparent benefit; similarly, changing its legal status; and the custodian is liable for what he spent from the mosque's wealth. He built in its courtyard in the countryside a shop for the purpose of prayer, they pray in it in congregation at all times; it has the ruling of a mosque; and a beam should not be placed on the wall of the mosque even if it is from its endowments. End. And in it from the section on dislike: Even if there is an entrance to the mosque from a waqf house, there is no harm for the imam to enter through this door because it is reported that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, used to enter from his room to the mosque. (If) he has a specific place in the mosque that he regularly occupies, and someone else occupied it; al-Awza'i said he has the right to displace him; but according to us (Hanafis) he does not have that, and it is disliked to allocate a specific place in the mosque for oneself because it spoils humility. The dust of the mosque has no sanctity when collected, but it has sanctity when spread.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage contains several independent rulings concerning mosque administration and etiquette. First, if candles sent to a mosque burn partially, the imam or mu'adhdhin cannot take the remainder without the donor's permission, even if custom allows. Second, creating new windows in a mosque is disapproved based on Ibn Mas'ud's narration. Third, renting out parts of the mosque for commercial activities is invalid if it harms the mosque's purpose. Fourth, removing a wall between two mosques to merge them is not allowed unless there is clear benefit. Fifth, a shop built in the courtyard for prayer with congregation takes the ruling of a mosque. Sixth, a beam should not be placed on the mosque wall even if from its waqf. Seventh, the imam may enter through a private door from a waqf house, following the Prophet's practice. Eighth, no one has the right to claim a specific spot in the mosque for regular use; it is disliked to allocate a place for oneself. Ninth, the dust of the mosque when gathered (swept) has no sanctity, but when spread on the floor, it retains its sanctity as part of the mosque.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or common practice that is explicit and widely recognized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can leftover candles be taken by the imam or mu'adhdhin?
A) Always, because they are in the mosque.
B) Only with the donor's permission or if custom is explicit.
C) Only if the candles are completely burnt.
D) Only if the donor is deceased.

Key Takeaway
Donated mosque items remain the donor's property; any leftover cannot be appropriated without permission, unless explicit custom allows.

Prohibited Mosque Modifications
Multiple rulings on mosque modifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify at least three prohibited or invalid modifications to a mosque and explain the underlying reason.

Core Idea
Mosque modifications are invalid when they conflict with the mosque's purpose or lack clear benefit.

The passage presents several rulings on mosque modifications: creating windows, renting space, merging mosques, building shops for prayer, and placing beams. Each ruling aims to preserve the sanctity and intended function of the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify each modification mentioned
List the five modifications: (1) creating new windows, (2) renting mosque space for commercial use, (3) removing a wall between two mosques to merge them, (4) building a shop in the courtyard for prayer, (5) placing a beam on the mosque wall.
Each has a different ruling.
2
Determine the ruling for each
(1) Creating windows: makruh. (2) Renting space: batil (invalid). (3) Removing wall: not allowed unless clear benefit. (4) Building shop for prayer: valid, takes hukm of mosque. (5) Placing beam: not allowed even if from mosque waqf.
Note the exception: the shop built exclusively for prayer is valid and treated as a mosque.
3
Understand the rationale
Modifications that detract from the mosque's primary purpose (prayer) or introduce worldly concerns are prohibited or invalid. The shop exception shows that if the activity is purely for prayer, it retains mosque ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية من آخر الوقف : بعث شمعاً في شهر رمضان إلى مسجد فاحترق وبقي منه ثلثه أو دونه ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذ بغير إذن الدافع ولو كان العرف في ذلك صريح في ذلك فله ذلك اهـ. وفيها : وكرهوا إحداث الطاقات في المساجد روي ذلك عن ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه. قيم الجامع القديم أجر موضعاً تحت ظلة الباب لبعض الصكاكين لا يصح . لا يجوز إزالة الحائط التي بين المسجدين ليجعلهما واحداً إذا لم يكن فيه مصلحة ظاهرة، وكذا رفع صفته ويضمن القيم ما أنفق فيه من مال المسجد. بنى في فنائه في الرستاق دكاناً لأجل الصلاة يصلون فيه بجماعة كل وقت فله حكم المسجد ولا يوضع الجذع على جدار المسجد وإن كان من أوقافه اهـ. وفيها من الكراهية ولو كان إلى المسجد مدخل من دار موقوفة لا بأس للإمام أن يدخل هذا الباب لأنه أن روي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يدخل من حجرته إلى المسجد. له في المسجد موضع معين يواظب عليه وقد شغله غيره قال الأوزاعي له أن يزعجه وليس له ذلك عندنا ويكره تخصيص مكان في المسجد لنفسه لأنه يخل بالخشوع . لا حرمة لتراب المسجد إذا جمع وله حرمة إذا بسط .

Imam's Entrance and Allocating Spots in the Mosque
Rulings on imam's entrance and spot allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the imam using a private door from a waqf house and on claiming a specific spot in the mosque for regular prayer.

Core Idea
The imam may enter through a waqf house door, but no one may claim a permanent spot as their own in the mosque.

The passage addresses two separate issues: first, the permissibility of the imam entering the mosque through a door that connects to a waqf house, following the Prophet's example; second, the ruling on habitually occupying a specific spot and whether one has the right to displace another who takes it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First ruling: Imam can enter through a door from a waqf house. Second ruling: No one has the right to claim a specific spot; it is disliked to allocate a spot for oneself.
2
Understand the first ruling: imam's door
The text cites the Prophet entering from his room to the mosque as precedent. This indicates that such access is permissible and does not violate the mosque's sanctity.
This applies when the door leads from a waqf property.
3
Analyze the second ruling: spot allocation
Al-Awza'i held that the regular occupant can displace a newcomer, but the Hanafi school disagrees. The passage states that allocating a specific place is disliked because it reduces khushu' (humility). The regular has no right to evict.
This ruling promotes equality and humility in the mosque.

Sanctity of Mosque Dust: Spread vs Collected
Ruling on the sanctity of mosque dust

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the sanctity of mosque dust when it is spread on the floor versus when it is swept up and collected.

Core Idea
Mosque dust is sacred only when spread on the floor; collected dust has no sanctity.

The final sentence of the passage addresses a subtle but important point: the dust of the mosque retains its sanctity only as long as it remains part of the mosque structure (i.e., spread on the floor). Once it is gathered, whether for cleaning or disposal, it loses its special status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states of mosque dust
The dust can be in two states: spread (مبسوط) on the floor, or collected (مجموع) after sweeping.
2
Apply the ruling to each state
When spread: it has sanctity (حرمة). When collected: it has no sanctity.
Sanctity here means it is considered part of the mosque and cannot be treated disrespectfully.
3
Understand the legal consequence
Collected dust can be discarded without special treatment, whereas spreading dust (e.g., from construction) should be handled with care if it might end up on the mosque floor.

Source Text
